Agency Necessitates, Receptivity Permits: Resolving the Contradiction
Second argument using necessity vs. possibility and resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that agency implies necessity while receptivity implies possibility, and how the author resolves the apparent contradiction by distinguishing between the two relations.

Core Idea
Agency necessitates; receptivity only allows.

The author presents a second argument: a complete agent must necessitate its effect, while a recipient only allows the effect to occur. These two relations seem contradictory, but the author shows that a thing can be necessary for something as an agent but not as a recipient, resolving the conflict.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Identify the two relations: agency (فاعلية) implies necessity (وجوب), receptivity (قبول) implies possibility (إمكان).
The key terms are الوجوب and الإمكان.
2
Understand the objection
An objector argues that receptivity only implies general possibility (الإمكان العام), which means 'not impossible'. This does not contradict necessity. Another says it is specific possibility (الإمكان الخاص).
Note the distinction between general and specific possibility.
3
Follow the author's answer
The author concedes the point but explains that even if general possibility, it is a broader concept that can include specific possibility, unlike definite necessity. The final resolution: it is permissible for one thing to be necessary as an agent but not as a recipient.
This shows no real contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد بعد تسليم كون القبول أثراً بأنا لا نسلم أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد، على أنه لو صح ذلك لزم أن لا يكون الواحد قابلاً لشيء وفاعلاً لآخر. فإن دفع باختلاف الجهة، فإن الفاعلية لذاته وقابليته باعتبار تأثره عما يوجد المقبول. قلنا : فليكن حال القابلية والفاعلية للشيء الواحد أيضاً كذلك. فإن قيل : الشيء لا يتأثر عن نفسه. قلنا : أول المسألة، ولم لا يجوز باعتبارين كالمعالج لنفسه. فإن قيل : الكلام على تقدير اتحاد الجهة. قلنا : فيكون لغواً إذ لا اتحاد جهة أصلاً. الثاني : أن نسبة الفاعل إلى المفعول بالوجوب، ونسبة القابل إلى المقبول بالإمكان. لأن الفاعل التام للشيء من حيث هو فاعل يستلزمه، والقابل له لا يستلزمه بل يمكن حصوله فيه، فيكون قبول الشيء للشيء، وفاعليته له متنافيين لتنافي لازميهما أعني الوجوب والإمكان. واعترض بأنه إنما هي إمكان عام، لأن معنى قابلية الشيء للشيء أنه لا يمتنع حصوله فيه، وهو لا ينافي الوجوب. وقيل : بل معناه أنه لا يمتنع حصوله فيه ولا عدم حصوله. وهو معنى الإمكان الخاص، ولو فرضناه الإمكان العام، فليس معناه أحد نوعيه، أعني الوجوب بل معناه مفهومه الأعم بحيث يحتمل الإمكان الخاص فينا في تعين الوجوب الذي لا يحتمله. والجواب بعد تسليم ذلك : أنه يجوز أن يكون الشيء واجباً للشيء من حيث كونه فاعلاً له غير واجب من حيث كونه قابلاً له.
After conceding that receptivity is an effect, [we say:] we do not accept that from one only one proceeds. Moreover, if that were true, it would necessitate that one thing cannot be receptive to something and agent for another. If it is rebutted by [appeal to] difference of aspect, that agency is due to its essence and receptivity is due to being affected by that from which the received thing comes, we say: then let the case of receptivity and agency for one thing also be likewise. If it is said: a thing is not affected by itself. We say: that is the very matter at issue; why is it not permissible under two considerations, like one treating oneself? If it is said: the discussion is on the assumption of unity of aspect. We say: then it is futile, since there is no unity of aspect at all. Second: that the relation of the agent to the act is by necessity, and the relation of the recipient to the received is by possibility. For the complete agent of a thing, insofar as it is agent, necessitates it, while the recipient of it does not necessitate it but rather its occurrence in it is possible. Thus, a thing's being a recipient of something and its being an agent for it are contradictory due to the contradiction of their concomitants, namely necessity and possibility. And it was objected that it is only general possibility, because the meaning of a thing's being receptive of something is that its occurrence in it is not impossible, and that does not contradict necessity. And it was said: rather its meaning is that its occurrence in it is not impossible and its non-occurrence is not impossible. That is the meaning of specific possibility. Even if we assume it is general possibility, its meaning is not one of its two types, namely necessity, but rather its meaning is the broader concept that admits specific possibility, unlike definite necessity that does not admit it. The answer, after conceding that, is that it is permissible for a thing to be necessary for something insofar as it is an agent for it, but not necessary insofar as it is a recipient for it.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of the philosophers' principle 'from one only one proceeds.' The author first concedes that receptivity is an effect but denies the principle itself, arguing that if it held, one thing could not be both agent and recipient of different things. The philosopher may defend by saying agency is by essence and receptivity by being affected from another; the author retorts that the same can be said for one thing being both agent and recipient under different aspects. When the philosopher objects that a thing cannot be affected by itself, the author says 'that's the issue' and gives an example of self-treatment. When the philosopher demands unity of aspect, the author calls it futile since aspects are different. Then the author provides a second argument: agency implies necessity (wujub) of the effect from the agent, while receptivity implies possibility (imkan) of the received in the recipient—these two relations are incompatible. The objector counters that receptivity only implies general possibility (imkan 'amm), which does not contradict necessity. Another says it is specific possibility (imkan khas). The author answers that even if general possibility, it is a broader concept that does not exclude specific possibility, but necessity does. The final resolution: it is permissible for one thing to be necessary in terms of being an agent for something but not necessary as a recipient—so the contradiction is resolved.

Key Terms
الوجوب
الإمكان
الإمكان العام
الإمكان الخاص
الفاعلية
القبول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessity and possibility are mutually exclusive in all respects, so one thing cannot be both necessary and possible.
Correction: They are contradictory only when applied to the same relation; a thing can be necessary as an agent and possible as a recipient.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relation of the agent to the act?
A) By possibility
B) By necessity
C) By general possibility
D) By specific possibility

Key Takeaway
A thing can be necessary for something as an agent, but not necessary as a recipient, resolving the apparent contradiction between necessity and possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are both an agent of your deeds and a recipient of Allah’s decree. Which role do you tend to emphasize in your daily life, and how does that imbalance affect your trust in Allah? Commit today to consciously acknowledge both roles in one action.
?
When you fail in an act of obedience, do you think of yourself as a failed agent or as a needy recipient? How does the lesson’s resolution that these roles are not contradictory change your view of repentance? Write down one specific repentance action you will take today.
?
The passage shows that necessity and possibility can coexist in different relations. In your relationship with Allah, where do you feel a sense of ‘necessity’ (e.g., you must pray) and where do you feel ‘possibility’ (e.g., that your prayer may not be accepted)? How can this understanding increase your humility and hope? Resolve to pray one extra prayer today with this awareness.

The Ash'ari Doctrine on Corporeal Powers: No Causal Efficacy
The author states the Ash'ari doctrine and refutes the philosophers' view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that corporeal powers have no inherent causal effect and how they refute the philosophers' arguments.

Core Idea
Corporeal powers do not cause effects; Allah creates them directly.

This lesson presents a key Ash'ari doctrine: the denial of causal efficacy to physical powers like fire's heat. The author states the position and then refutes the philosophers' counterarguments. You will see how the Ash'ari view upholds divine transcendence and the direct creation of all effects by Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the overall argument. Notice the contrast between the Ash'ari and philosopher views.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the Two Positions
Identify the Ash'ari assertion (no effect for corporeal powers, no need for position) and the philosophers' view (effects require specific position and are finite).
Look for key terms like 'الوضع' and 'تناهي الفعل'.
3
Analyze the Refutation
Examine how the author responds to the philosophers' arguments, especially the denial that power is proportional to size.
Consider why the spheres' motion does not refute the Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس
لا تأثير للقوى الجسمانية
قال: لا تأثير للقوى الجسمانية عندنا فلا يشترط في ظهور أفعالها الوضع، ولا يمتنع دوامها لخلق الله تعالى. وعند الفلاسفة يشترط في تأثيرها الوضع للقطع بأن النار لا تسخن إلا ما له بالنسبة إليه وضع مخصوص، ويلزم تناهي فعلها بحسب الشدة، وهو ظاهر ويتوسط المدة والعدة، لأن القسرى يختلف باختلاف القابل والطبيعي باختلاف الفاعل لتفاوت الصغير والكبير في المفارقة وتساويهما في القبول، لأن المفارقة للطبيعة، التي هي في الكبير أقوى والقبول للجسمية التي هي فيهما على السواء. فاذا فرض في حركتيهما الاتحاد في المبدأ بتفاوت الجانب الآخر ويلزم التناهي، ولا ينتقض بحركة الأفلاك لأنها تستند إلى إرادات من نفوسها المجردة. والجواب بعد تسليم التأثير منع كون القوة بقدر الحجم.
The Fifth Investigation: No Effect for Corporeal Powers. He said: Corporeal powers have no effect according to us, so a specific position is not necessary for the appearance of their acts, nor is their continuance impossible, due to Allah’s creation. According to the philosophers, a specific position is necessary for their effect, because it is certain that fire does not heat except that which has a specific position relative to it, and its effect must be finite in intensity, which is apparent, and intermediate in time and number, because the forceful differs according to the difference of the recipient, and the natural differs according to the difference of the agent, due to the difference between small and large in separation and their equality in reception, because separation belongs to nature, which is stronger in the large, and reception belongs to corporeality, which is equal in both. So if one assumes in their two motions unity in the starting point with difference in the other aspect, finitude is necessary. And this is not invalidated by the motion of the spheres because they are based on volitions from their abstract souls. The answer, after conceding effect, is the denial that power is proportional to size.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a theological investigation into whether corporeal powers have real causal efficacy. The author begins by stating the Ash'ari position: corporeal powers (like fire’s heat) have no real effect; rather, God creates the effects directly. Therefore, no specific spatial position (wad') is required for their acts, and they can continue indefinitely if God wills. In contrast, the philosophers (falasifa) hold that such powers do cause effects, requiring a specific position—fire only heats what is appropriately placed. They argue that the effect must be finite in intensity, and intermediate in time and number, because the strength of the effect differs based on the recipient’s capacity (for natural motion) and the agent’s power (for voluntary motion). They illustrate with a small and large object: the larger has a stronger nature for separation but equal corporeality for reception, so if both start from the same point, the larger’s motion will be stronger but finite. The motion of the spheres is an exception because it comes from abstract souls, not corporeal powers. The author’s response: even if we concede that corporeal powers have effects, we deny that power is proportional to size—thus breaking the philosophers’ deduction of finitude.

Key Terms
القوى الجسمانية
الوضع
تناهي الفعل
القسرى
الطبيعي
النفوس المجردة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ash'aris deny all causality; they assign all effects directly to God, not to inherent powers in bodies.
Correction: Ash'aris affirm that Allah is the sole creator of effects; corporeal powers are only occasions, not causes.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position stated in the passage, what is required for corporeal powers to produce effects?
A) A specific spatial position.
B) The power must be proportional to size.
C) Nothing; God creates the effects directly.
D) The effect must be finite in intensity.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari doctrine holds that no created power has independent causal efficacy; all effects are directly from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers believed that fire's heat requires a specific position to act. In your daily life, do you find yourself assuming that your circumstances (your 'position') determine what you can achieve? How would your heart change if you truly believed that Allah creates every effect directly, regardless of your position? What is one situation today where you can consciously attribute the outcome to Allah alone?
?
The Ash'ari response denies that power is proportional to size. Think of an area where you rely on your own strength—knowledge, wealth, or status. Do you subconsciously think that more 'size' gives you more power to effect good? How does this passage expose that as a form of hidden shirk? What would it look like for you to depend entirely on Allah's creation of effects, even when you feel strong?
?
The philosophers exempted the motion of the spheres because it comes from abstract souls. Are there parts of your life—your worship, your intentions, your good deeds—that you treat as 'exempt' from the general rule, believing they have inherent power to bring about spiritual results? How does this passage call you to repent of that assumption and to see all good as directly from Allah?

Bodily Powers: Sunni Position and Philosophers' Objection
Contrasts Sunni and philosophical views on bodily causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni denial of bodily causal efficacy and the philosophers' counterargument based on position and finitude.

Core Idea
Bodily powers do not cause; God creates all effects.

This passage presents a key theological debate: do bodily powers (like fire's heat or water's wetness) have true causal influence? Sunni theologians deny they have any effect, while philosophers argue they do, requiring specific spatial positioning. The passage explores the implications of each view and the philosophers' reasoning for finitude.

Guided Path
1
Read the Sunni stance
Identify the opening statement that denies bodily powers have any effect and note that neither position nor continuation is a condition.
The phrase 'لا تاثير للقوى الجسمانية عندنا' clearly states the Sunni position.
2
Examine the philosophers' objection
Read the philosophers' claim that effect requires a specific position and that the action must be finite in intensity, duration, and number.
The example of fire heating only what is positioned relative to it illustrates the position condition.
3
Understand the analogy
Grasp the distinction between forced motion (differs by recipient) and natural motion (differs by agent) and the implications for large vs. small bodies.
Equality in reception but difference in separation leads to finitude when starting from the same point.
4
See the Sunni answer
Note the brief response: conceding influence but denying proportionality of power to volume, thus blocking the finitude argument.
The answer is concise and focuses on the key assumption of proportionality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس
لا تأثير للقوى الجسمانية
قال: لا تاثير للقوى الجسمانية عندنا فلا يشترط في ظهور أفعالها الوضع، ولا يمتنع دوامها لخلق الله تعالى. وعند الفلاسفة يشترط في تأثيرها الوضع للقطع بأن النار لا تسخن إلا ما له بالنسبة إليه وضع مخصوص، ويلزم تناهي فعلها بحسب الشدة، وهو ظاهر ويتوسط المدة والعدة، لأن القسرى يختلف باختلاف القابل والطبيعي باختلاف الفاعل لتفاوت الصغير والكبير في المفارقة وتساويهما في القبول، لأن المفارقة للطبيعة، التي هي في الكبير أقوى والقبول للجسمية التي هي فيهما على السواء. فاذا فرض في حركتيهما الاتحاد في المبدأ بتفاوت الجانب الآخر ويلزم التناهي، ولا ينتقض بحركة الأفلاك لأنها تستند إلى إرادات من نفوسها المجردة. والجواب بعد تسليم التأثير منع كون القوة بقدر الحجم.
Fifth chapter: No effect for bodily powers. He said: There is no effect for bodily powers according to us, so their position is not a condition for the appearance of their actions, nor is their continuation impossible because of Allah's creation. According to the philosophers, position is a condition for their effect, because it is certain that fire does not heat except what has a specific position relative to it, and their action must be finite in intensity, which is apparent, and it is mediated by duration and number, because [the effect of] compulsion differs according to the recipient, and natural [effect] differs according to the agent, due to the difference between the small and the large in separation and their equality in reception, because separation belongs to nature, which is stronger in the large, and reception belongs to corporeality, which is equal in both. So if in their two motions unity in the beginning is assumed, with difference in the other aspect, finitude is necessary, and this is not refuted by the motion of the spheres because they are based on volitions from their abstract souls. The answer, after conceding influence, is to deny that the power is proportional to volume.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents the fundamental Sunni position on bodily causality and the philosophers' counterargument. The author states that for Sunni Muslims (أهل السنة), bodily powers have no true effect; their actions are directly created by God. Therefore, no specific position (الوضع) is required for their effects to appear, and their continuation (like the eternal pleasures of paradise or punishment of hell) is not impossible. The philosophers, however, assert that such powers do have an effect, and they require a specific spatial position (الوضع المخصوص) for the effect to occur—for example, fire only heats what is positioned relative to it. The philosophers further argue that the action of such powers must be finite in intensity, duration, and number. They support this with an analogy: in forced (القسر) motion, the effect depends on the recipient, whereas in natural motion, it depends on the agent. A large and a small body share equal capacity to receive (القبول), but differ in their natural separation (المفارقة). Assuming both start motion from the same point but differ in the other aspect, finitude follows. The objection that the motion of the spheres seems continuous is rejected because the spheres are not subject to bodily powers but are directed by the wills of abstract souls. The final brief answer from the Sunni perspective is to concede that bodily powers have some influence but to deny that this influence is proportional to the volume of the body—thus the finitude argument does not apply.

Key Terms
القوى الجسمانية
الوضع
التأثير
التناهي
القسر
الطبيعي
القابل
المفارقة
أهل السنة
الفلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Sunni position denies any causality whatsoever, but it affirms that God creates effects at the occasion of bodily actions, not that bodies have independent causal power.
Correction: The Sunni position does not deny causality absolutely; it denies that bodies are independent causes. Effects are directly created by Allah, but bodies are occasions or loci for that creation.

Checkpoint
According to the Sunni position stated in the passage, what is not required for the appearance of actions attributed to bodily powers?
A) The existence of a cause
B) A specific position (الوضع)
C) The intention of the agent
D) The presence of a recipient

Key Takeaway
The Sunni view denies independent causal power to bodies, attributing all effects to Allah, while the philosophers insist on real efficacy tied to spatial positioning and finitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience success from your own efforts—your strength, your skill, your intelligence—do you truly believe that Allah creates the outcome at that moment, or do you secretly credit yourself? What would it mean to live as if every result is directly from Him, not from your abilities?
?
The philosophers argue that without a specific position, fire cannot heat. In your daily life, you rely on consistent cause-effect patterns: food satisfies hunger, medicine heals illness. How would your trust in Allah change if you saw these not as natural necessities but as Allah's direct, moment-by-moment creation?
?
The Sunni response concedes some influence but denies proportionality to volume. Is there an area in your worship where you assume that 'more effort' guarantees 'more reward'? Examine your heart: do you believe that quantity of deeds determines closeness to Allah, or is it the divine will that grants acceptance regardless of volume?

Contrasting Views: Sunna and Philosophers on Bodily Powers
Contrasts Sunni and philosopher views on bodily powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental difference between the Sunni and philosophical positions on the causality of bodily powers, and the philosophers' requirement of spatial position for their effect.

Core Idea
Bodily powers: Sunni deny effect, philosophers affirm with positional condition.

Before diving into the philosophers' intricate proof of finitude, we must first understand how they differed from the Sunni position. The Sunni school, affirming that all actions are directly from God, does not ascribe independent causal efficacy to bodily powers, nor require any particular spatial position. The philosophers, by contrast, insist that bodily powers do have real effects, but those effects depend on a specific spatial relationship. This lesson clarifies these foundational contrasts.

Guided Path
1
Read the context: Sunni position
The opening lines restate the Sunni view: no independent effect for bodily powers, no positional requirement, and no hindrance to continued action like paradise and hell.
This sets up the contrast.
2
Introduce the philosophers' view
Notice how the philosophers affirm effect and require position, with examples of fire and sun.
The examples show that position is crucial.
3
Reflect on the significance
Why does the positional condition matter? It implies that bodily powers are not absolute but limited, leading to the finitude argument in the next lesson.
This lesson sets the stage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون باستناد الممكنات إلى الله تعالى ابتداء لا يثبتون للقوى الجسمانية تأثيراً، ولا يشترطون في ظهور الأفعال المترتبة عليها بخلق الله تعالى وضعاً، ولا يمنعون دوام تلك الأفعال، كما في نعيم الجنة وعذاب الجحيم. وأما الفلاسفة فيثبتون لها تأثيراً، ويشترطون فيه الوضع لا تسخن كل شيء، والشمس لا يضيء بها كل شيء بل ماله بالنسبة إليهما وضع مخصوص، بل ويقطعون بأنه يلزم تناهيها بحسب العدة والمدة والشدة، بأن يكون عدد آثارها وحركاتها متناهياً، وكذا زمانها في جانبي الازدياد والانتقاص، بأن لا تزداد إلى غير نهاية ولا تنتقص إلى غير نهاية، وذلك أن المتصف حقيقة بالتناهي واللاتناهي هو الكم المتصل أو المنفصل والقوة التي محلها متناه إنما تتصف بهما باعتبار كمية المتعلق، أعني الحركات والآثار الصادرة عنها، إما كمية انفصالية وهي عدد الآثار، وإما كمية اتصالية وهي زمان الآثار وهو مقدار ممكن فيه فرض التناهي واللاتناهي في جانب الازدياد الاختلاف بحسب المدة، وفي جانب الانتقاض وهو الاختلاف بحسب الشدة. بيان ذلك ان الشيء الذي يتعلق به شيء ذو مقدار أو عدد كالقوى التي يصدر عنها عمل متصل في زمان أو أعمال متوالية لها عدد، فبفرض النهاية واللانهاية فيه يكون بحسب مقدار ذلك العمل، أو عدد تلك الأعمال، والذي بحسب المقدار يكون أما مع فرض وحدة العمل واتصال زمانه، أو مع فرض الاتصال في العمل نفسه لا من حيث يعتبر وحدته أو كثرته، وبهذه الاعتبارات تصير القوى أصنافاً ثلاثة: الأول: قوى تفرض صدور عمل واحد عنها في أزمنة مختلفة كرماة تقطع سهامهم مسافة محدودة في أزمنة مختلفة ولا محالة يكون التي زمانها أقل أشد
Those who say that contingent things depend on God Almighty from the beginning do not affirm any effect for bodily powers, and they do not require a position for the appearance of the actions that follow from them by God's creation, and they do not prevent the continuation of those actions, as in the bliss of Paradise and the punishment of Hell. As for the philosophers, they affirm an effect for them, and they require in it a position—fire does not heat everything, and the sun does not illuminate everything, but only that which has a specific position relative to them; indeed they are certain that their finitude is necessary with respect to number, duration, and intensity: that the number of their effects and motions is finite, and similarly their time in the two directions of increase and decrease—that they do not increase indefinitely nor decrease indefinitely. This is because what is truly described as finite and infinite is continuous or discrete quantity, and the power that resides in a finite thing is only described as such by virtue of the quantity of its correlate, namely the motions and effects proceeding from it—either a discrete quantity, which is the number of effects, or a continuous quantity, which is the time of the effects; it is a magnitude in which finitude and infinitude can be assumed in the aspect of increase, which is variation in duration, and in the aspect of decrease, which is variation in intensity. The explanation of that is: a thing that is related to something that has magnitude or number, like powers from which proceeds a continuous action in time or successive actions having a number—by assuming finitude or infinitude in it, it will be according to the magnitude of that action or the number of those actions. That which is according to magnitude is either with the assumption of the unity of the action and continuity of its time, or with the assumption of continuity in the action itself regardless of considering its unity or multiplicity. By these considerations powers become three kinds: First: powers from which a single action is assumed to proceed in different times, like archers whose arrows cover a limited distance in different times; inevitably, the one whose time is less is more intense.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage first restates the Sunni position for context, then presents the philosophers' detailed argument for the finitude of bodily powers. The philosophers assert that bodily powers have real effects, but require a specific spatial position. They further argue that such powers must be finite in three respects: number (عدد) of effects, duration (زمان) of effects, and intensity (شدة) of the effect. The reasoning is that what is truly finite or infinite is quantity (الكم), either discrete (number) or continuous (duration). A power residing in a finite body is itself finite, so its effects must be finite in quantity. The argument then explains that the quantity of effects can be considered either as discrete (number of effects) or continuous (duration). In either case, one can assume finiteness or infiniteness. The author then clarifies that when considering a power that produces a single action in different times, the power is measured by the time taken: a shorter time implies greater intensity. This leads to a classification of powers into three kinds. The passage mentions only the first kind: powers that produce a single action over different times (like archers shooting arrows the same distance in different times; the one with shorter time is more intense). The other two kinds are not included in this window.

Key Terms

الفلاسفة
الوضع

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Sunni view denies all secondary causality.
Correction: The Sunni view denies independent causal efficacy to created things, but affirms that Allah creates actions through them as instruments; they do not have effect by themselves.

Checkpoint

C) Because fire only heats by the will of God
D) Because fire is not hot

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position denies independent effect to bodily powers, while the philosophers affirm effect but require a specific spatial position.

?

?
The philosophers require a specific position for effects to occur — this conditions their view of causality. In your relationship with Allah, do you subconsciously condition His response on your own position (e.g., being in a certain place, having performed certain acts)? What does the Sunni view challenge in that assumption?
?
If bodily powers have no independent effect, then every breath you take is a direct creation of Allah. Are you living as if that is true, or do you rely on natural mechanisms as if they were self-sufficient? What is one step you can take today to affirm total reliance on Him?

The Philosophers' Proof: Bodily Powers Must Be Finite
Presents the philosophers' proof of finitude and first kind of power classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational basis for the philosophers' claim that bodily powers are finite in number, duration, and intensity, and their preliminary classification of powers.

Core Idea
Finitude of powers proven through three quantities: number, duration, intensity.

The philosophers built their case for the finitude of bodily powers on a logical analysis of quantity. Since bodily powers reside in finite bodies, their effects must be finite in number, duration, and intensity. This lesson unpacks that argument and introduces the first of three kinds of powers derived from this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument's premise
The passage states that what is truly finite/infinite is quantity—either discrete (number) or continuous (duration).
This is the conceptual foundation.
2
Understand the three aspects
Finitude applies to number of effects, duration (time), and intensity (strength).
Intensity is related to decrease in time.
3
Explore the classification
The passage classifies powers into three kinds based on the quantity considered. The first kind is described: a power producing a single action in different times, where shorter time means greater intensity.
The archer example illustrates this.

القائلون باستناد الممكنات إلى الله تعالى ابتداء لا يثبتون للقوى الجسمانية تأثيراً، ولا يشترطون في ظهور الأفعال المترتبة عليها بخلق الله تعالى وضعاً، ولا يمنعون دوام تلك الأفعال، كما في نعيم الجنة وعذاب الجحيم. وأما الفلاسفة فيثبتون لها تأثيراً، ويشترطون فيه الوضع لا تسخن كل شيء، والشمس لا يضيء بها كل شيء بل ماله بالنسبة إليهما وضع مخصوص، بل ويقطعون بأنه يلزم تناهيها بحسب العدة والمدة والشدة، بأن يكون عدد آثارها وحركاتها متناهياً، وكذا زمانها في جانبي الازدياد والانتقاص، بأن لا تزداد إلى غير نهاية ولا تنتقص إلى غير نهاية، وذلك أن المتصف حقيقة بالتناهي واللاتناهي هو الكم المتصل أو المنفصل والقوة التي محلها متناه إنما تتصف بهما باعتبار كمية المتعلق، أعني الحركات والآثار الصادرة عنها، إما كمية انفصالية وهي عدد الآثار، وإما كمية اتصالية وهي زمان الآثار وهو مقدار ممكن فيه فرض التناهي واللاتناهي في جانب الازدياد الاختلاف بحسب المدة، وفي جانب الانتقاض وهو الاختلاف بحسب الشدة. بيان ذلك ان الشيء الذي يتعلق به شيء ذو مقدار أو عدد كالقوى التي يصدر عنها عمل متصل في زمان أو أعمال متوالية لها عدد، فبفرض النهاية واللانهاية فيه يكون بحسب مقدار ذلك العمل، أو عدد تلك الأعمال، والذي بحسب المقدار يكون أما مع فرض وحدة العمل واتصال زمانه، أو مع فرض الاتصال في العمل نفسه لا من حيث يعتبر وحدته أو كثرته، وبهذه الاعتبارات تصير القوى أصنافاً ثلاثة: الأول: قوى تفرض صدور عمل واحد عنها في أزمنة مختلفة كرماة تقطع سهامهم مسافة محدودة في أزمنة مختلفة ولا محالة يكون التي زمانها أقل أشد

Philosophers' Proof of Finite Physical Effects
Presents the philosophers' argument that physical forces have finite effects in number, duration, and intensity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that physical forces produce finite effects in number, duration, and intensity, and the reasoning behind this claim.

Core Idea
Physical forces cannot produce infinite effects because their locus is finite.

The philosophers argue that since physical forces reside in finite bodies, their effects must be limited. This lesson presents their classification of forces and the proof that coercive and natural effects are necessarily finite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the full translation. Note the contrast between theologians and philosophers.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three types of finitude
Find where the passage states that effects are finite in 'al-ʿidda, al-mudda, al-shidda' (number, duration, intensity). Understand what each means.
3
Understand the classification of forces
The passage divides forces into three types based on the nature of their effects. Notice the examples of archers for single action in different times and for successive actions.
The second type (intensity) is not separately numbered but is implied.
4
Follow the proof for number and duration
The philosophers focus on proving finitude in number and duration, as infinite intensity is obviously impossible. Analyze the argument about coercive effect varying with the size of the recipient, and natural effect varying with the agent.
This is the core of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون باستناد الممكنات إلى الله تعالى ابتداء لا يثبتون للقوى الجسمانية تأثيراً، ولا يشترطون في ظهور الأفعال المترتبة عليها بخلق الله تعالى وضعاً، ولا يمنعون دوام تلك الأفعال، كما في نعيم الجنة وعذاب الجحيم. وأما الفلاسفة فيثبتون لها تأثيراً، ويشترطون فيه الوضع، ولا تسخن النار كل شيء، والشمس لا يضيء بها كل شيء بل ماله بالنسبة إليهما وضع مخصوص، بل ويقطعون بأنه يلزم تناهيها بحسب العدة والمدة والشدة، بأن يكون عدد آثارها وحركاتها متناهياً، وكذا زمانها في جانبي الازدياد والانتقاص، بأن لا تزداد إلى غير نهاية ولا تنتقص إلى غير نهاية. وذلك أن المتصف حقيقة بالتناهي واللاتناهي هو الكم المتصل أو المنفصل، والقوة التي محلها متناه إنما تتصف بهما باعتبار كمية المتعلق، أعني الحركات والآثار الصادرة عنها، إما كمية انفصالية وهي عدد الآثار، وإما كمية اتصالية وهي زمان الآثار، وهو مقدار ممكن فيه فرض التناهي واللاتناهي في جانب الازدياد الاختلاف بحسب المدة، وفي جانب الانتقاض وهو الاختلاف بحسب الشدة. بيان ذلك أن الشيء الذي يتعلق به شيء ذو مقدار أو عدد كالقوى التي يصدر عنها عمل متصل في زمان أو أعمال متوالية لها عدد، فبفرض النهاية واللانهاية فيه يكون بحسب مقدار ذلك العمل، أو عدد تلك الأعمال، والذي بحسب المقدار يكون أما مع فرض وحدة العمل واتصال زمانه، أو مع فرض الاتصال في العمل نفسه لا من حيث يعتبر وحدته أو كثرته، وبهذه الاعتبارات تصير القوى أصنافاً ثلاثة: الأول: قوى تفرض صدور عمل واحد عنها في أزمنة مختلفة، كرماة تقطع سهامهم مسافة محدودة في أزمنة مختلفة، ولا محالة يكون التي زمانها أقل أشد قوة عن التي زمانها أكثر، ويجب من ذلك أن يقع عمل غير المتناهية لا زمان. والثالث: قوى يفرض صدور أعمال متوالية عنها مختلفة بالعدد، كرماة يختلف عدد رميهم، ولا محالة يكون التي يصدر عنها عدد أكثر أقوى من التي يصدر عنها عدد أقل. ويجب من ذلك أن يكون لعمل غير المتناهية عدد غير متناه. فالاختلاف الأول بالشدة، والثاني بالمدة، والثالث بالعدة. ولما كان امتناع اللاتناهي بحسب الشدة، وهو أن يقع الأثر في الزمان الذي هو في غاية القصر بل في الآن ظاهر الامتناع أن تقع الحركة إلا في زمان قابل للانقسام بحيث تكون القوة التي توقع الحركة في نصف ذلك الزمان أشد تأثيراً، اقتصروا على بيان امتناع اللاتناهي بحسب العدة والمدة، فقالوا: لا شك أن التأثير القسري يختلف باختلاف القابل المقسور، بمعنى أنه كلما كان أكبر كان تحريك القاسر له أضعف لكون معاوقته وممانعته أكبر وأقوى، لأنه إنما يعاوق بحسب طبيعته، وهي في الجسم الكبير أقوى منها في الجسم الصغير لاشتماله على مثل طبيعة الصغير مع الزيادة. فإذا فرضنا تحريك جسم بقوته جسماً من مبدأ معين، ثم تحريكه جسماً آخر مماثلاً له بحسب الطبيعة، وأكبر منه بحسب المقدار بتلك القوة بعينها، ومن ذلك المبدأ بعينه، لزم أن يتفاوت منتهى حركة الجسمين، بأن تكون حركة الأصغر أكثر من حركة الأكبر لكون المعاوقة فيه أقل، فبالضرورة تنتهي حركة الأكبر، ويلزم منها انتهاء حركة الأصغر، لأنها إنما تزيد على حركة الأكبر بقدر زيادة مقداره على مقدار الجسم إذ المفروض أنه لا تفاوت إلا بذلك. والتأثير الطبيعي يختلف باختلاف الفاعل، بمعنى أنه كلما كان الجسم أعظم مقداراً كانت الطبيعة فيه أقوى وأكثر آثاراً.
Those who hold that contingent things are attributed to God Almighty primarily do not affirm any effect for physical forces, and they do not stipulate a position for the appearance of the actions consequent upon them by God's creation, and they do not prevent the continuation of those actions, like the bliss of Paradise and the torment of Hell. As for the philosophers, they affirm for them an effect, and they stipulate position in it; fire does not heat everything, and the sun does not illuminate everything, but only what has a specific position relative to them. Moreover, they are certain that their finitude necessarily follows in terms of number, duration, and intensity, such that the number of their effects and movements is finite, and likewise their time in the directions of increase and decrease, such that they neither increase without end nor decrease without end. This is because that which is truly described by finitude and infinitude is the continuous or discrete quantity, and the force whose locus is finite is only described by them in consideration of the quantity of the related thing, namely the movements and effects issuing from it—either discrete quantity, which is the number of effects, or continuous quantity, which is the time of the effects, and it is a magnitude in which finitude and infinitude can be hypothesized in the direction of increase—the variation according to duration—and in the direction of decrease—which is the variation according to intensity. The explanation of that is that a thing to which something possessing magnitude or number is related, like forces from which a continuous action in time or successive actions having number issue, then by hypothesizing finitude and infinitude in it, it is according to the magnitude of that action or the number of those actions. And that which is according to magnitude is either with the hypothesis of the unity of the action and the continuity of its time, or with the hypothesis of continuity in the action itself, not insofar as its unity or multiplicity is considered. By these considerations, forces become three types: First: forces from which a single action is hypothesized to issue in different times, like archers whose arrows cut a limited distance in different times, and inevitably the one whose time is less is stronger in force than the one whose time is more, and it follows from that that an action of the infinite has no time. Third: forces from which successive actions differing in number are hypothesized to issue, like archers whose number of shots differs, and inevitably the one from which a greater number issues is stronger than the one from which a lesser number issues. And it follows from that that an action of the infinite has an infinite number. So the first difference is by intensity, the second by duration, and the third by number. And since the impossibility of infinity according to intensity—that the effect occurs in the time that is extremely short, nay, in the instant—is manifestly impossible (motion occurs only in time divisible such that the force that causes motion in half that time is more effective), they confined themselves to demonstrating the impossibility of infinity according to number and duration, and said: There is no doubt that coercive effect differs according to the coerced recipient, meaning that the larger it is, the weaker the coercer's movement of it because its resistance and opposition are greater and stronger, for it resists according to its nature, which in a large body is stronger than in a small body because it encompasses the nature of the small along with an increase. So if we suppose moving a body with its force from a given starting point, then moving another body similar in nature but larger in magnitude with that same force and from that same starting point, it necessarily follows that the endpoints of the motion of the two bodies differ, such that the motion of the smaller is greater than the motion of the larger because the resistance in it is less. So necessarily, the motion of the larger ends, and from it follows the ending of the motion of the smaller, because it only exceeds the motion of the larger by the amount of the increase of its magnitude over the magnitude of the body, since it is assumed that there is no difference except that. And natural effect differs according to the agent, meaning that the greater the body in magnitude, the stronger and more effective its nature is.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument that physical forces have finite effects. It begins by contrasting the theologians, who deny any real causal efficacy to physical bodies and attribute all effects directly to God, with the philosophers, who affirm that physical bodies have genuine causal power, subject to conditions like position. The philosophers then argue that the effects of such forces must be finite in three respects: number (عدة), duration (مدة), and intensity (شدة). They explain that finitude and infinitude properly apply to quantity, either discrete (number of effects) or continuous (time of effects). Since the locus of a force is finite, its effects are finite in quantity. They classify forces into three types: (1) those whose single action takes different times—the shorter the time, the stronger the force; (2) those whose successive actions differ in number—the greater the number, the stronger the force; (3) forces that produce actions varying in intensity (though not explicitly numbered as second, it is implied). The philosophers then focus on proving the impossibility of infinite duration and number, since infinite intensity is obviously impossible (an effect cannot occur in an instant, as all motion requires time). They argue that coercive effect varies with the recipient: a larger body offers more resistance, so the same force moves a smaller body further. Therefore, the motion of a larger body must end, and consequently the smaller's motion also ends, because the difference is only due to size. Similarly, natural effect varies with the agent: a larger body has a stronger nature and produces more effects. The argument concludes that physical forces cannot have infinite effects in duration or number.

تناهي
لاتناهي
شدة
مدة
عدة
تأثير قسري
تأثير طبيعي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the philosophers deny divine causation entirely.
Correction: The philosophers affirm secondary causation in bodies while still recognizing God as the ultimate cause.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' argument that all physical effects are finite in number, duration, and intensity constitutes a key challenge to the possibility of an eternal physical world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your own body is a finite locus of physical forces. All the actions you produce—every effort, every word, every deed—are finite in number, duration, and intensity. What are you building in this life that will survive beyond your body's finite existence? Are you investing in what the theologians affirm: eternal actions that depend not on your limited power but on God's direct creation, like the bliss of Paradise?
?
The philosophers argue that a force cannot produce an infinite number of actions. Think of the things you hope to achieve: your career, relationships, legacy. Do you assume your own efforts can create lasting impact, or do you recognize that any lasting effect must come from a source beyond your finite physical capacity? How does this lesson expose the limits of your self-reliance?
?
The theologians deny any real causal power to physical forces, attributing all effects to God alone. In your daily life, do you act as if your own choices and efforts truly cause outcomes, or do you live with the constant awareness that every effect is directly from God? Which perspective would lead to greater gratitude, humility, and trust in God's sovereignty?

Three Differences Among Forces and the Exclusion of Intensity
Classifies three types of differences in forces and excludes intensity from proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three ways forces can differ—intensity, duration, and number—and why theologians exclude intensity when proving the impossibility of infinite forces.

Core Idea
Forces differ in intensity, duration, and number; infinite intensity is self-evidently impossible.

In this passage from Sharh al-Maqasid, the author categorizes the differences among forces into three distinct types: intensity, duration, and number. He then explains that the impossibility of infinite intensity is so obvious that it needs no proof, which is why scholars focus their arguments on duration and number. This lesson will guide you through these categories and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without diving into analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the logical flow: it first discusses forces acting in time, then forces with multiple acts, then lists the three types.
2
Identify the three types of differences
Locate where the author names the three differences: intensity (الشدة), duration (المدة), and number (العدة). Understand how each is defined in the preceding sentences.
Intensity relates to how quickly the effect occurs; duration to the time span; number to the quantity of effects.
3
Understand why intensity is excluded
Read the final part where the author explains that the impossibility of infinite intensity is obvious because motion requires divisible time. Ask yourself why that makes a proof unnecessary.
Theologians then restrict their proofs to duration and number.
4
Connect to the broader theological context
Reflect on why proving the impossibility of infinite forces matters in kalam: it supports the createdness of the world and the existence of a Creator.
This passage is part of a larger discussion on causation and the limits of created forces.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوة عن التي زمانها أكثر، ويجب من ذلك أن يقع عمل غير المتناهية لا زمان. والثالث قوى يفرض صدور أعمال متوالية عنها مختلفة بالعدد كرماة يختلف عدد رميهم، ولا محالة يكون التي يصدر عنها عدد أكثر أقوى من التي يصدر عنها عدد أقل. ويجب من ذلك أن يكون لعمل غير المتناهية عدد غير متناه، فالاختلاف الأول بالشدة، والثاني بالمدة والثالث بالعدة. ولما كان امتناع اللاتناهي بحسب الشدة وهو أن يقع الأثر في الزمان الذي هو في غاية القصر بل في الآن ظاهر الامتناع أن تقع الحركة إلا في زمان قابل للانقسام بحيث تكون القوة التي توقع الحركة في نصف ذلك الزمان أشد تأثيراً، اقتصروا على بيان امتناع اللاتناهي بحسب العدة والمدة.
A force which [acts] in a longer time, and it is necessary from that that an act occur [that is] infinite without time. And the third [type] is [for] forces from which successive acts of different number are supposed to issue, like archers whose number of shots differs, and inevitably the force from which a greater number issues is stronger than that from which a lesser number issues. And it is necessary from that that an infinite act have an infinite number. So the first difference is by intensity, the second by duration, and the third by number. And since the impossibility of infinity according to intensity – which is that the effect occurs in the time that is at the extreme of shortness, rather in an instant – is manifestly impossible, since motion only occurs in a time divisible into parts such that the force that produces motion in half of that time is more intense in effect, they restricted themselves to demonstrating the impossibility of infinity according to number and duration.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage classifies the differences among forces into three types: intensity (shidda), duration (mudda), and number (ʿidda). The author explains: if a force acts in a longer time, an infinite act would require no time, which is impossible. For forces producing multiple acts, a force that produces more acts is stronger. Thus, infinite acts would require infinite number. The author then states that the impossibility of infinite intensity is obvious because motion cannot occur in an instant but only in divisible time; thus they focus on demonstrating the impossibility of infinity in duration and number.

Key Terms

المدة
العدة
اللاتناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do the theologians restrict their discussion to the impossibility of infinity in duration and number rather than intensity?
A) Because the impossibility of infinite intensity is manifestly obvious
B) Because they did not know about intensity
C) Because intensity is not relevant to their proofs
D) Because they proved intensity separately

Key Takeaway
Forces differ by intensity, duration, and number; infinite intensity is self-evidently impossible, so theologians prove it for duration and number only.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that infinite intensity in a created force is impossible because motion requires time. Consider your own life: in what moments do you act as if your energy or effort can be infinitely intense, expecting instant results? How does recognizing your finitude change how you approach your daily tasks and long-term goals? Commit to one area where you will be more patient with time.
?
The archer analogy tells us that a greater number of acts requires a stronger force. Reflect on your own 'acts'—your deeds, responsibilities, and distractions. Are you trying to produce too many outcomes with limited strength? What would it look like to focus your 'force' on fewer, more important acts? Write down one thing you will stop doing to concentrate your energy.
?
The theologians accepted the impossibility of infinite intensity as self-evident—they didn't need to prove it. Are there uncomfortable truths about your own limitations that you already know but avoid confronting (like your finite time, energy, or ability)? What is one such self-evident truth you have been ignoring, and how will you face it starting today?

Proof of Finitude of Motion: Forced and Natural
Presents a proof that motion must be finite using forced and natural motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the kalam argument using forced and natural motion demonstrates that all motion must be finite.

Core Idea
All motion, whether forced or natural, must eventually come to an end.

Sanusi presents a detailed proof that motion cannot be infinite. He examines two fundamental types of motion—forced and natural—and shows that in both cases, the motion of a body must cease. This lesson walks you through the logical steps of this proof, clarifying the interplay between the mover, the moved, and their properties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow. Notice that the argument has two main parts: forced motion and natural motion.
2
Understand the forced motion argument
Focus on the first half that deals with forced motion. The key idea is that resistance increases with size, so a larger body moves less distance. The smaller body's motion ends because it only exceeds the larger in proportion to its size.
Remember: the force is the same and the starting point is the same.
3
Understand the natural motion argument
Now look at the second half on natural motion. Here, natural strength grows with size, but receptivity to motion is equal. So the smaller body exhausts its motion first, and the larger must also stop because its motion is proportional.
The logic is inverted: in forced motion resistance dominates; in natural motion strength dominates.
4
See how both combine to prove finitude
The proof concludes that both forced and natural motions are finite. The author explains the logical structure of the argument, showing that the two cases together cover all motion.
The 'guidance' at the end clarifies the structure of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقالوا لا شك أن التأثير القسري يختلف باختلاف القابل المقسور، بمعنى أنه كلما كان أكبر كان تحريك القاسر له أضعف لكون معاوقته وممانعته أكبر وأقوى، لأنه إنما يعاوق بحسب طبيعته، وهي في الجسم الكبير أقوى منها في الجسم الصغير لاشتماله على مثل طبيعة الصغير مع الزيادة، فإذا فرضنا تحريك جسم بقوته جسماً من مبدأ معين، ثم تحريكه جسماً آخر مماثلاً له بحسب الطبيعة، وأكبر منه بحسب المقدار بتلك القوة بعينها، ومن ذلك المبدأ بعينه لزم أن يتفاوت منتهى حركة الجسمين بأن تكون حركة الأصغر أكثر من حركة الأكبر لكون المعاوقة فيه أقل، فبالضرورة تنتهي حركة الأكبر ويلزم منها انتهاء حركة الأصغر، لأنها إنما تزيد على حركة الأكبر بقدر زيادة مقداره على مقدار الجسم إذ المفروض أنه لا تفاوت إلا بذلك. والتأثير الطبيعي يختلف باختلاف الفاعل. بمعنى أنه كلما كان الجسم أعظم مقداراً كانت الطبيعة فيه أقوى وأكثر آثاراً، لأن القوى الجسمانية المتشابهة إنما تختلف باختلاف محالها بالصغر والكبر، لكونها متجزئة بتجزئتها. وأما في قبول الحركة فالصغير والكبير فيه متساويان لأن ذلك للجسمية، وهي فيهما على السوية، فإذا فرضنا حركة الصغير والكبير بالطبع من مبدأ معين لزم التفاوت في الجانب الآخر ضرورة أن الجزء لا يقوى على ما يقوى عليه الكل فتنقطع حركة الصغير، ويلزم منه انتهاء حركة الكبير لكونهما على نسبة جسميهما، فقوله لتفاوت الصغير والكبير بيان للاختلاف القسري باختلاف القابل، وقوله وتساويهما في القبول بيان لعدم اختلاف الطبيعي باختلاف القابل. وقوله فإذا فرض في حركتيهما أي حركتي الصغير والكبير شروع في تقدير الدليل وهو جامع للقسري والطبيعي، ولم يقع في كلام القوم إلا بطريق التفصيل على ما شرحناه.
And they said: There is no doubt that forced effect differs according to the difference of the subject that is forced, meaning that the larger it is, the weaker the mover's moving of it, because its opposition and resistance are greater and stronger, since it opposes according to its nature, and this is stronger in the large body than in the small body because it contains the nature of the small plus an increase. So if we suppose a body being moved by its force from a certain starting point, then another body similar in nature but larger in magnitude being moved by that same force from that same starting point, it necessarily follows that the endpoints of the motion of the two bodies differ, such that the motion of the smaller is more than the motion of the larger because the opposition in it is less. So necessarily the motion of the larger ends, and from that follows the end of the motion of the smaller, because it only exceeds the motion of the larger by the amount of the increase of its magnitude over the magnitude of the body, since it is assumed that there is no difference except that. And natural effect differs according to the agent, meaning that the larger the body in magnitude, the stronger and more effective the nature in it, because similar corporeal forces only differ according to the difference of their loci in smallness and largeness, since they are divided by their division. But as for receiving motion, the small and large are equal in it because that is due to corporeality, which is equally in both. So if we suppose the natural motion of the small and large from a certain starting point, difference in the other side necessarily follows, because the part does not have the power for what the whole has power for, so the motion of the small ceases, and from that follows the end of the motion of the large because they are in proportion to their bodies. So his statement 'the difference of the small and large' is an explanation of the forced difference according to the subject, and his statement 'their equality in reception' is an explanation of the absence of difference of the natural according to the subject. And his statement 'so if it is supposed in their two motions'—meaning the motions of the small and large—is the beginning of constructing the proof, which combines the forced and natural, and it has not occurred in the statements of the people except by way of detail as we have explained.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed proof that all motion—whether forced or natural—must be finite. For forced motion (al-ta'thīr al-qasrī), the author explains that resistance increases with size, so a large body moved by a given force will stop first, and the smaller body's motion must also stop because its increase in distance is only proportional to its size. For natural motion (al-ta'thīr al-ṭabīʿī), natural strength increases with size, but receptivity to motion is equal, so the smaller body's motion ceases first, forcing the larger to stop as well. The author then clarifies the logical structure of the argument.

Key Terms
التأثير القسري
التأثير الطبيعي
القابل
الفاعل
المقسور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof only applies to forced motion, not to natural motion.
Correction: The passage explicitly includes both forced and natural motion, showing that both lead to finitude. Natural motion is treated separately, with a different mechanism, but the conclusion is the same.

Checkpoint

B) All motion must be finite
C) Forced motion never ends
D) Natural motion is infinite

Key Takeaway
By analyzing both forced and natural motion, the kalam argument establishes that all motion must be finite, supporting the world's temporal origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your life is a series of motions—your ambitions, your efforts, your very heartbeat. This lesson proves that all such motion is inevitably finite. If your time on earth were to end within the next hour, what would you wish you had done more of? What would you regret? Let that urgency shape your next action.
?
The proof shows that even natural motion—the movement that flows from your own nature—has limits. In which area of your life have you been living as if your strength, influence, or lifespan were unlimited? What is one concrete step you can take today to acknowledge your finitude and turn to the One who is infinite?
?
Both forced and natural motions end. Your external achievements and your inner drives will all cease. Does this truth fill you with despair or with hope? If, like the kalam philosophers, you use this to affirm a beginning, what does that say about your Creator who initiates motion without being moved?

Objection from Celestial Motions and Its Answer
Objection to the proof using celestial motions, with answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the kalam objection from celestial motions challenges arguments for the temporal origination of bodies and why the response distinguishes volitional celestial motion from physical motion.

Core Idea
Celestial motions are volitional, not purely physical, so they do not refute the proof.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection from the motion of celestial spheres. The objector argues that celestial motions are infinite and based on corporeal forces, which would undermine the proof that bodies require a cause for their motion. The response distinguishes between physical and volitional motion, preserving the proof's validity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection broadly
Read the Arabic passage and translation once to grasp the overall structure: a general objection followed by four detailed points and an answer.
Notice that the passage uses 'إجمالاً' for general and 'تفصيلاً' for detailed.
2
Break down the four detailed objections
Identify each of the four detailed objections starting with 'لم لا يجوز' and understand the logical progression: eternity of forces, non-unity of beginning, speed differences, and quantity differences.
The fourth objection uses an analogy of the moon and Saturn.
3
Examine the answer
Focus on the answer to the first objection: it says celestial motions are volitional (إرادية) based on wills and partial intellections, not purely physical. This explains why the proof does not apply.
The answer only addresses the first objection; the others are left unanswered in this passage.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Ask yourself: How does calling celestial motions 'volitional' change the argument? Why does this distinction matter for proving the temporal origination of the universe?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن نوقض الدليل إجمالاً بالحركات الفلكية، فإنها مع عدم تناهيها عندهم مستندة إلى قوى جسمانية لها إدراكات جزئية . إذ التعقل الكلي لا يكفي في جزئيات الحركة على ما سيجيء. وتفصيلاً بأنه لم لا يجوز أن تكون القوى الجسمانية أزلية لا يكون لحركاتها مبدأ ولو سلم فإنا لا نسلم إمكان ما فرضتم من اتحاد المبدأ، بل مبدأ حركة الأصغر أصغر من مبدأ حركة الأكبر. ولوسلم فلم لا يجوز أن يكون التفاوت الذي لابد منه هو التفاوت بالسرعة والبطء بأن يكون حركة الأصغر أسرع في القسرية، وأبطأ في الطبيعية من غير انقطاع . ولو سلم فالتفاوت بالزيادة والنقصان لا يوجب الانقطاع كما إذا فرضنا لحركة فلك القمر، وفلك زحل مبدأ من موازاة نقطة معينة من الفلك الأعظم، فإن دورات القمر أضعاف دورات زحل مع عدم تناهيهما . أجيب عن الأول : بأن حركات الأفلاك إرادية مستندة إلى إرادات وتعقلات جزئية مستندة إلى نفوسها المجردة في ذواتها المقارنة في أفعالها.
For the proof is objected to in general by [appeal to] celestial motions, for they, despite their infinity in their view, are based on corporeal forces that have partial perceptions. Since universal intellection does not suffice for particulars of motion, as will come. And in detail: Why is it not permissible that corporeal forces be eternal, having no beginning for their motions? And if it is granted, we do not grant the possibility of what you supposed of the unity of the beginning; rather, the beginning of the motion of the smaller is smaller than the beginning of the motion of the larger. And if it is granted, why is it not permissible that the difference that is necessary be the difference in speed and slowness, such that the motion of the smaller is faster in the forced [case] and slower in the natural [case] without cessation? And if it is granted, difference in increase and decrease does not necessitate cessation, as if we suppose for the motion of the sphere of the moon and the sphere of Saturn a beginning from the alignment of a certain point of the highest sphere, for the revolutions of the moon are multiples of the revolutions of Saturn despite their non-finiteness. The first [objection] is answered: That the motions of the spheres are volitional, based on wills and partial intellections, based on their abstract souls that accompany [their bodies] in their actions.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the previous proof by appealing to celestial motions (al-ḥarakāt al-falakiyya). The objector challenges the proof generally, noting that celestial motions are infinite yet based on corporeal forces with partial perceptions. Then four detailed objections are given: 1) Corporeal forces could be eternal with no beginning. 2) Even if the proof is accepted, we do not accept the assumption of a single starting point. 3) Difference could be in speed rather than cessation. 4) Difference in quantity does not imply cessation, as seen in the motions of the moon and Saturn. The answer to the first objection is that celestial motions are volitional (irādiyya), dependent on wills and partial intellections, and not purely physical, so the proof does not apply.

Key Terms
الحركات الفلكية
القوى الجسمانية
إرادية
إدراكات جزئية
التعقل الكلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that celestial motions refute the proof because they are infinite and physical, making the argument invalid.
Correction: The answer clarifies that celestial motions are volitional (إرادية), not purely physical, so the proof for temporal origination of physical bodies remains valid.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Celestial motions are volitional, not purely physical, so they do not contradict the proof that all bodies are temporally originated.

?

?

Forced vs Natural Motion in Bodies
Author explains why forced motion differs by size but natural motion does not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why forced motion differs with body size while natural motion is equal, based on the principles of divisible physical powers and bodiliness (al-jismiyya).

Core Idea
Forced motion varies with size; natural motion does not.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid tackles a key puzzle in physics and theology: why do small and large objects behave differently when pushed, but fall at the same rate? The author uses the concepts of physical powers (al-quwa al-jismaniyya) and bodiliness (al-jismiyya) to resolve this. Let's read his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice the technical terms: القوى الجسمانية, القسري, الطبيعي, الجسمية.
Pay attention to the three statements beginning with 'وقوله'.
2
Identify the two types of motion
The passage distinguishes forced motion (القسري) from natural motion (الطبيعي). Forced motion differs by size; natural motion does not. Find the reasons given for each.
3
Understand the hypothetical proof
The author presents a thought experiment: if small and large bodies start natural motion from the same point, the small stops first because its part cannot do what the whole can. This shows both forced and natural aspects.
This proof demonstrates the relationship between forced and natural motion.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how the proof combines forced and natural motion. Earlier scholars only detailed them separately. Why does the author combine them?
This shows the author's integrated approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن القوى الجسمانية المتشابهة إنما تختلف باختلاف محالها بالصغر والكبر، لكونها متجزئة بتجزئتها. وأما في قبول الحركة فالصغير والكبير فيه متساويان لأن ذلك للجسمية، وهي فيهما على السوية، فإذا فرضنا حركة الصغير والكبير بالطبع من مبدأ معين لزم التفاوت في الجانب الآخر ضرورة أن الجزء لا يقوى على ما يقوى عليه الكل فتنقطع حركة الصغير، ويلزم منه انتهاء حركة الكبير لكونهما على نسبة جسميهما، فقوله : لتفاوت الصغير والكبير بيان للاختلاف القسري، باختلاف القابل، وقوله : وتساويهما في القبول بيان لعدم اختلاف الطبيعي باختلاف القابل. وقوله : فإذا فرض في حركتيهما أي حركتي الصغير والكبير شروع في تقدير الدليل وهو جامع للقسري والطبيعي، ولم يقع في كلام القوم إلا بطريق التفصيل على ما شرحناه.
Because the similar physical powers only differ according to the difference of their places in smallness and bigness, due to their being divided by their division. And as for the acceptance of motion, the small and the big in it are equal because that is due to bodiliness (al-jismiyya), and it is in both equally. So if we assume the motion of the small and the big naturally from a given starting point, inequality would be necessary in the other side necessarily because the part cannot do what the whole can do, so the motion of the small would cease, and from that it follows that the motion of the big ends because they are in proportion to their bodies. So his statement: 'the difference of the small and the big' is an explanation of the forced difference according to the difference of the receiver. And his statement: 'their equality in acceptance' is an explanation of the lack of difference of the natural according to the difference of the receiver. And his statement: 'so if he supposes in their two motions' meaning the motions of the small and the big is a beginning to formulate the proof, and it combines the forced and the natural, and it did not occur in the speech of the people except by way of detail as we have explained.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage continues a proof about physical powers. It explains that similar bodily powers differ only by the size of their location because they are divisible with division. However, for acceptance of motion, small and large bodies are equal because motion is due to jismiyya (bodiliness), which is the same in both. If we suppose a natural motion from a common starting point, inequality would arise in the opposite direction: the part cannot do what the whole can, so the small's motion stops, implying the large's motion also stops due to proportion. The author clarifies that forced motion differs by the receiver, but natural motion does not differ by the receiver. The proof combines both forced and natural aspects and was not presented by earlier scholars except in detailed form.

Key Terms
القوى الجسمانية
القسري
الطبيعي
الجسمية
القابل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes all powers are divisible in the same way for forced and natural motion.
Correction: The author clarifies that forced motion differs due to divisible powers, but natural motion is equal due to bodiliness, which is not divisible in this context.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are small and large bodies equal in natural motion?
A) Because they have the same bodily nature (jismiyya).
B) Because the small cannot resist.
C) Because forced motion differs.
D) Because the proof combines both types.

Key Takeaway
Small and large bodies share the same bodiliness, so natural motion is equal; forced motion differs because powers are divided.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In your own life, do you judge your abilities by your 'size'—your status, wealth, or strength—believing that your capacity for good actions (like charity or worship) is limited by your resources? This lesson shows that in what truly matters (bodiliness for natural motion), all are equal. Where have you let your perceived smallness prevent you from undertaking a spiritual 'natural motion' that is equally available to you? What will you start doing today that you previously avoided because you thought you were too small?
?
The passage distinguishes between forced motion (driven by external powers) and natural motion (from the essence). Which areas of your life are being driven by external pressures—societal expectations, peer influence, or fear—rather than your natural disposition as a believer (fitra)? How can you realign your actions to come from your inner essence rather than external force?
?
If the small and the large are equal in natural motion because of their shared bodiliness, what does that imply about the spiritual equality of believers before Allah? Do you treat others who are smaller in worldly terms as equals in faith and potential? Are there people you have dismissed as less capable of spiritual growth because of their 'size'? How will you change your interaction with them this week?
