The Aggregate Argument Against Infinite Regress
First proof against infinite regress with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the aggregate argument against infinite regress and how it necessitates an external Necessary Cause.

Core Idea
The collection of all effects is itself a possible being requiring an external cause, terminating the regress.

Theologians developed several proofs to show that an infinite regress of causes is impossible. This lesson presents the first proof: the aggregate argument. It demonstrates that if causes and effects stretch back endlessly without a first cause, the entire set of effects forms a possible being that must have a cause outside of itself. The author then addresses a key objection about the reality of such aggregates and refines the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage with its translation. Notice the structure: it begins with the aggregate argument, then an objection, then a detailed reply.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Follow the logic of the aggregate argument
Identify why the aggregate of all effects must be existent and possible. Then see why its cause cannot be itself or a part of it, forcing an external cause.
The key move: the aggregate is possible because it depends on its possible parts.
3
Understand the objection and answer
The objection claims the aggregate might be merely conceptual. The answer clarifies that it means a real collection, not a single extra entity. Then the author refines the notion of the 'cause of the whole chain'.
This reply prevents a common misunderstanding and strengthens the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجوا على بطلان التسلسل بوجوه : الأول : أنه (٦) لو تسلسلت العلل والمعلولات من غير أن ينتهي إلى علة محضة لا يكون معلولاً لشيء لكان هناك جملة مجموع الموجودة المعلول كل من أحادها واحد منها وتلك الجملة موجودة ممكنة (٨) أما الوجود فلانحصار أجزائها في الموجود ومعلوم أن المركب لا يعدم إلا بعدم شيء من أجزائه. وأما الإمكان فلافتقارها إلى جزئها الممكن ومعلوم أن المفتقر إلى الممكن لا يكون إلا ممكناً، ففي جعلها نفس الموجودات الممكنة تنبيه على أنها مأخوذة بحيث لا تدخل فيها المعدوم أو الواجب لا يقال المركب من الأجزاء الموجودة قد يكون اعتبارياً لا تحقق له في الخارج كالمركب من الحجر والإنسان ومن الأرض والسماء . لأنا نقول : المراد أنه (۱) ليس موجوداً واحداً يقوم به وجود غير وجودات الأجزاء. وإلا فقد صرحوا بأن المركب الموجود في الخارج قد لا يكون له حقيقة مغايرة لحقيقة الأحاد كالعشرة من الرجال وقد يكون إما مع صورة ممنوعة كالنبات من العناصر، وإما بدونها لا يزداد إلا هيئة (۲) اجتماعية كالسرير من الخشبات، وإذا كانت الجملة موجوداً ممكناً، فموجدها بالاستقلال. (٤) أما نفسها وهو ظاهر (۳) الاستحالة، وأما جزء منها، وهو أيضاً محال لاستلزامه كون ذلك الجزء علة لنفسه ولعلله لأنه لا معنى لإيجاد الجملة، إلا إيجاد الأجزاء التي هي عبارة عنها ولا معنى لاستقلال الموجد ( ) إلا استغناؤه عما سواه، وإما أمر خارج عنها، ولا محالة يكون موجدا لبعض الأجزاء، وينقطع إليه البتة (٥) سلسلة المعلولات لكون الموجد الخارج عن جميع الممكنات واجبا بالذات، ولا يكون ذلك البعض معلولاً لشيء من أجزاء الجملة لامتناع اجتماع العلتين المستقلتين على معلول واحد. إذ الكلام في المؤثر المستقل بالإيجاد فيلزم الخلف من وجهين لأن المفروض أن السلسلة غير منقطعة، وأن كل جزء منها معلول الجزء آخر، وبما ذكرنا من التقرير يندفع نقض الدليل تفصيلاً بأنه إن أريد بالعلة التي لا بد منها لمجموع السلسلة العلة التامة ، فلا نسلم إستحالة كونها نفس السلسلة وإنما يستحيل لو لزم تقدمها، وقد سبق أن العلة التامة للمركب لا يجب بل لا يجوز تقدمها إذ من جملتها الأجزاء التي هي نفس المعلول.
They argued against the invalidity of infinite regress with proofs: The first: that if the causes and effects regressed infinitely without ending in a pure cause that is not an effect of anything, then there would be an aggregate, a sum of existent effects, each of its individuals one of them, and that aggregate would be existent and possible. As for existence, because its parts are confined to the existent, and it is known that the composite ceases to exist only by the non-existence of some of its parts. And as for possibility, because of its need for its possible part, and it is known that that which needs the possible is only possible. So in making it the very possible existents is a reminder that it is taken such that the non-existent or the Necessary is not included in it. It cannot be said: the composite of existent parts may be merely mental, having no realization externally, like the composite of stone and human, and of earth and sky. For we say: what is meant is that it is not a single existent subsisting by an existence other than the existences of the parts. Otherwise, they have stated that the composite existing externally may not have a reality distinct from the reality of the individuals, like ten men; and it may be either with a prohibited form, like plants from elements, or without it, not adding except a social configuration, like a bed from planks. And when the aggregate is an existent possible, its independent existent is either itself—which is manifest impossibility—or a part of it—which is also impossible because it necessitates that part being a cause for itself and its causes; for there is no meaning to 'effecting the aggregate' except effecting the parts which constitute it, and no meaning to 'the independence of the effector' except its independence from others—or an external thing, and inevitably it will be an effector for some parts, and the chain of effects will cease at it utterly, because the effector external to all possibles is Necessary by essence, and that part is not an effect of any part of the aggregate due to the impossibility of two independent causes concurring on one effect. For the discussion is about the independent effective cause. So a contradiction follows from two aspects: because it is assumed that the chain is unbroken, and that every part of it is an effect of another part. And by what we have mentioned in the exposition, the refutation of the proof is repelled in detail: that if by 'the cause necessary for the whole chain' is meant the complete cause, we do not concede the impossibility of it being the chain itself; it would be impossible only if its priority were necessary, and it has preceded that the complete cause of a composite does not have to be prior — rather it is not permissible for it to be prior, because among its parts are the parts which are the effect itself.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument against the possibility of an infinite regress of causes and effects, followed by an objection and answer. The author states: 'They argued against infinite regress with proofs. The first: if causes and effects regress infinitely without ending in a pure cause that is not an effect, then there would be an aggregate consisting of all those effects. That aggregate would be existent (since it is composed of existent parts) and possible (since it depends on its parts which are possible). This aggregate, being possible, must have an independent cause. That cause cannot be the aggregate itself (since that would be self-causation), nor a part of it (since that part would be cause of itself and its own cause). Therefore the cause must be external to the aggregate. That external cause would be an effector of some parts, and the chain of effects would terminate at it, because that external cause, being beyond all possibles, is Necessary by essence. Moreover, that part caused by the external cause cannot be an effect of any other part in the aggregate, because two independent causes cannot concur on the same effect. This leads to a contradiction: the assumption was that the chain is unbroken and each part is caused by another. Thus the regress must end. An objection follows: someone might say that a composite of existent parts may be merely conceptual (اعتباري) with no external reality, like a composite of stone and human. The answer: what is meant is not that the aggregate has an existence over and above its parts; rather, such an aggregate is real in the sense of being a collection. The example of ten men or a bed shows that such aggregates exist externally as configurations. Since the aggregate is a possible existent, it requires an external cause as argued. Then the author adds a further refinement: if by 'necessary cause for the whole chain' one means the complete cause, we do not grant that it is impossible for the chain itself to be its own complete cause. It would be impossible only if the complete cause must be prior to its effect, but for a composite, the complete cause is not prior; rather its parts are simultaneous with the effect. This subtle point guards against a possible counterargument.

Key Terms
تسلسل
علة
معلول
جملة
ممكن
واجب
علة تامة
معلول محض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The aggregate of an infinite series is merely a mental concept and has no external reality, so it does not require a cause.
Correction: The aggregate exists externally as a collection of real parts; it is not a separate entity but a real configuration, as shown by examples like ten men or a bed.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument against infinite regress, why cannot the cause of the aggregate be a part of it?
A) Because that part would be an effect of itself.
B) Because that part would be external to the aggregate.
C) Because the aggregate is not a real existent.
D) Because the part would be a cause of the whole but not of itself.

Key Takeaway
The aggregate argument shows that an infinite regress of causes would form a possible aggregate requiring an external cause, thus disproving the possibility of an unending causal chain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, you rely on countless causes—your health, your job, your relationships. The aggregate argument forces you to admit that all these possible things together cannot be self-sufficient. Where in your heart do you still act as if they are? Today, what is one area you will consciously attribute to Allah as the sole independent cause?
?
The argument shows that the chain of effects must end in a Necessary Being. But do you live as if your own existence is merely a link in that chain, wholly dependent on Allah at every moment? Write down one specific thought or worry that you habitually feel responsible for solving on your own, and then silently affirm that only Allah truly brings it about.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

The Contingent Totality Requires an External Cause
Taftazani clarifies the composite and proves the need for an external cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a series of contingent beings, considered as a totality, cannot be self-causing and must have an external cause.

Core Idea
A chain of contingents, even as a whole, remains contingent and demands an independent cause outside itself.

Al-Taftazani begins by clarifying what is meant by a composite of existing parts. He then presents the core argument: if a totality of contingent beings exists, its cause must be either itself, a part of it, or something external. After eliminating the first two, he concludes that an external cause—the Necessary Being—is required.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of composite
Read the opening lines where Taftazani refines the notion of a composite and addresses the objection that some composites are merely conceptual.
Notice the examples: ten men, plant from elements, bed from wood.
2
Follow the three-fold division of possible causes
The author enumerates that the cause of the totality can be itself, a part, or external. He proves each impossibility except the external.
3
Grasp the conclusion: the external cause must be Necessary
The external cause, being independent and causing some parts, must be Necessary in essence and terminates the chain.
This is the foundation for the proof of God's existence.

واجباً لكون وجودها من ذاتها وكفى بهذا استحالة قلنا ممنوع وإنما يلزم لو لم يفتقر إلى جزئها الذي ليس نفس ذاتها سواء سمي غيرها أو لم يسم وإن أريد العلة الفاعلية فلا نسلم إستحالة كونها بعض أجزاء السلسلة وإنما يستحيل لو لزم كونها علة لكل جزء من أجزاء المعلول حتى نفسه وعلله، وهو ممنوع لجواز أن يكون بعض أجزاء المعلول المركب مستنداً إلى غير فاعله كالخشب من السرير سلمنا ذلك. لكن لا نسلم السلسلة يكون واجباً لجواز أن توجد سلاسل غير متناهية من علل سلسلة أن الخارج من و معلولات غير متناهية وكل منها يستند إلى علة خارجة عنها داخلة في أخرى من غير انتهاء إلى الواجب . ولو سلم لزم الانتهاء إلى الواجب، فلا يلزم بطلان التسلسل لجواز أن يكون مجموع العلل والمعلولات الغير المتناهية موجوداً ممكناً مستنداً إلى الواجب، وإجمالاً بأنه منقوض بالجملة التي هي عبارة عن الواجب وجميع الممكنات الموجودة فإن علتها ليست On the condition that it is taken such that the non-existent or the Necessary is not included in it. It cannot be said that the composite of existing parts may be conceptual, having no realization externally, like the composite of stone and man, and of earth and sky. For we say: what is meant is that it is not one existent that subsists through an existence other than the existences of the parts. Otherwise, they have stated that the composite existing externally may have no reality distinct from the reality of its units, like ten men, and may be either with a prohibited form like the plant from elements, or without it, adding only a social configuration like the bed from pieces of wood. And if the totality is a contingent existent, then its cause is independent. [Its cause is] either itself—and that is clearly impossible—or a part of it, which is also impossible because it necessitates that part being a cause of itself and of its causes, since there is no meaning to causing the totality except causing the parts that constitute it, and no meaning to the independence of the cause except its being independent of what is other than it—or something external to it, which necessarily causes some of the parts, and the chain of effects terminates in it because the cause external to all contingents is Necessary in essence, and that part is not an effect of any part of the totality due to the impossibility of two independent causes concurring on one effect. For the discussion concerns the independent agent of existence. So a contradiction follows from two aspects: because it is assumed that the chain is unbroken and that every part of it is an effect of another part. And by what we have mentioned of the exposition, the refutation of the proof is repelled in detail: that if by 'the cause without which the totality of the chain cannot be' is meant the complete cause, then we do not concede the impossibility of it being the chain itself; it is only impossible if its priority is necessary. And it has preceded that the complete cause of a composite does not necessarily, nay must not, be prior to it, since among its parts are the parts that are the very effect. For if it is said: then it would follow that it is Necessary because its existence is from itself, and this alone suffices as impossibility. We say: it is denied; it would only follow if it did not depend on its part which is not its own essence, whether it is called 'other than it' or not. And if by 'efficient cause' is meant, then we do not concede the impossibility of it being some parts of the chain; it is only impossible if it necessitates it being a cause of every part of the effect, including itself and its causes. And this is denied, because it is possible that some parts of the composite effect are dependent on other than its agent, like the wood of the bed. We concede that. But we do not concede that the chain would be Necessary, because it is possible that there exist infinite chains of causes of a chain that the external from ... and effects infinite, and each of them depends on a cause external to it included in another without terminating at the Necessary. And even if it is conceded, the termination at the Necessary would follow, so the invalidity of regress does not follow, because it is possible that the totality of infinite causes and effects is a contingent existent dependent on the Necessary. And in summary: that it is refuted by the totality which is an expression of the Necessary and all existing possibles; for its cause is not itself, nor a part of it, for what was mentioned, nor external to it, because that would necessitate, along with the multiplicity of the Necessary, the effectness of the Necessary and the concurrence of agents if it is a cause of every part of the totality, and one of the two things if it is a cause of some parts. And the way of repulsion is that we have stated that what is meant by 'cause' is the independent efficient agent of existence, and we took the totality as the very collection of all possibles such that every part of it is an effect of a part, so the external to it would be nothing but the Necessary. And the least that follows from its independence in causality is that there exists in the totality a part that is not an effect of another part but specifically of the external, and that is the meaning of termination. And it is impossible for the independent in causality to be a part of the totality, because it would necessitate it being a cause of itself and of its causes, in realization of the meaning of independence. For if the cause of some parts were something else, the attainment of the totality would also depend on it, so neither of them would be independent. And this is unlike the composite composed of the Necessary...
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation by Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani of an objection concerning the impossibility of infinite regress and the necessity of an external cause for a series of contingent beings. The argument unfolds in several stages.

The author begins by clarifying the notion of a composite (مركب) of existing parts: it does not include the non-existent or the Necessary Being. He then addresses a potential objection that a composite may be merely conceptual (اعتباري) with no external reality, like a stone-man or earth-sky composite. The response is that what is meant here is that the composite is not a single existent with an existence over and above the existences of its parts. Externally existing composites may either have no reality distinct from their units (e.g., ten men) or have either a prohibited form (e.g., plant from elements) or a merely configurational unity (e.g., a bed from pieces of wood). If the totality (الجملة) is a contingent existent, it must have an independent cause (موجد بالاستقلال). This cause cannot be the totality itself (that would be circular), nor a part of it (that would make the part a cause of itself and of its causes, which is impossible), so it must be something external. That external cause, being the cause of some parts, terminates the chain of effects, because it is the Necessary in essence (واجب بالذات) and the part it causes is not caused by any other part of the totality (to avoid two independent causes concurring on one effect).

The discussion then moves to a detailed refutation of an attempted counterargument. It is said that if by 'cause of the whole chain' we mean the 'complete cause' (علة تامة), then it is not impossible for the chain itself to be its own complete cause, since the complete cause of a composite need not precede it (its parts are the very effect). The author rejects this: the complete cause's non-priority is allowed, but the issue is the regress of efficient causes. If by 'cause' we mean the 'efficient cause' (علة فاعلية), then it is not impossible for a part of the chain to act as the efficient cause of some other parts, as long as that part does not cause itself or its own causes. However, even if we concede that, the chain as a whole cannot be Necessary (واجب) because there could be infinite series of causes without terminating at the Necessary. But then the objection that the totality of all such series is contingent and depends on the Necessary Being still holds. The author then addresses a potential counterexample: the composite of the Necessary Being and all possibles. Its cause cannot be itself, nor a part (the Necessary is not a part), nor external (that would imply a second Necessary). The repulsion is that in our argument, 'cause' means the independent efficient agent, and we considered the totality of all possibles where every part is caused by another part, so the external to the series is the Necessary. The non-existence of an external cause would mean that some part of the series is itself independent, which is impossible because it would have to cause itself and its causes. Finally, the author distinguishes this from the composite of the Necessary and possibles, implying that the Necessary is not part of the chain of effects.

Key Terms
المركب (al-murakkab)
الجملة (al-jumla)
الموجد بالاستقلال (al-mūjid bil-istiqlāl)
الواجب بالذات (al-wājib bi-l-dhāt)
الممكن (al-mumkin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The whole series of contingent beings could be self-sufficient without an external cause.
Correction: The author shows that the totality, as a contingent existent, cannot cause itself or be caused by a part; therefore, an external cause is necessary.

Checkpoint
Why cannot a part of the totality be its independent cause?
A) Because that part would be cause of itself.
B) Because that part would be cause of its own causes.
C) Both A and B.
D) Because the part is not external.

Key Takeaway
The only possible cause for a chain of contingent beings is an external Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen that the entire chain of contingent beings—including you—needs an external, independent cause. If that cause is Allah, then your existence depends on Him every moment. Does your daily life reflect that total dependence, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What specific change would show that you truly internalized this?
?
The argument proves that no part of the universe can be the ultimate cause. Think about something you rely on for security—health, money, a relationship. In light of this lesson, why is that thing incapable of being your ultimate support? What would it mean to shift your reliance entirely to the Necessary Being?
?
Taftazani concludes that the external cause is Necessary in Essence—His existence is not borrowed. Now consider your own faith: is it built on evidence that can be shaken, or on a conviction that is independent of circumstances? If everything you knew about Allah were taken away (your upbringing, your community), would you still have certainty? What are you doing to anchor your faith in the Necessary, not the contingent?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

Refuting Objections to the Proof from Infinite Regress
Taftazani refutes objections and defends the proof of an external cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes objections that the series could be its own complete cause or that an infinite regress avoids the need for an external cause.

Core Idea
Even if the series is infinite, it remains contingent and requires an external Necessary Being.

Al-Taftazani now addresses detailed objections. He distinguishes between complete cause and efficient cause, showing that the series cannot be its own complete cause without becoming necessary, and that an infinite regress of causes still depends on an external cause. He also counters an objection based on the composite of the Necessary and possibles.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection about the complete cause
The objector claims the chain could be its own complete cause. Taftazani denies this because it would entail priority issues and necessity.
2
Examine the objection about the efficient cause
The objector says a part of the chain could be the efficient cause of other parts. Taftazani shows this leads to the part causing itself and its causes.
3
Consider the possibility of infinite chains
The objector suggests infinite chains without termination. Taftazani replies that the totality of infinite chains is still contingent and depends on an external Necessary.
4
Analyze the final counterexample and its repulsion
The objector raises the composite of the Necessary and possibles. Taftazani clarifies that the Necessary is not part of the chain, so the argument stands.
This reinforces the need for an external independent cause.

نفسها، ولا جزءاً منها لما ذكر، ولا خارجاً عنها لاستلزامه مع تعدد الواجب معلولية الواجب واجتماع المؤثرين إن كان علة لكل جزء من أجزاء الجملة، وأحد الأمرين إن كان علة لبعض الأجزاء ووجه الاندفاع أنا قد صرحنا بأن المراد بالعلة الفاعل المستقل بالإيجاد وأخذنا الجملة نفس جميع الممكنات بحيث يكون كل جزء منها معلولاً لجزء ، فلم يكن الخارج عنها إلا واجباً، وأقل ما لزم من استقلاله بالعلية أن يوجد في الجملة جزء لا يكون معلولاً لجزء آخر بل للخارج خاصة، وهو معنى الانقطاع ولم يمكن أن يكون المستقل بالعلية جزءاً من الجملة للزوم كونه علة لنفسه ولعلله تحقيقاً بمعنى الاستقلال، إذ لو كان الموجد لبعض الأجزاء شيئاً آخر لتوقف حصول الجملة عليه أيضاً، فلم يكن أحدهما مستقلاً، وهذا بخلاف المجموع المركب من الواجب
On the condition that it is taken such that the non-existent or the Necessary is not included in it. It cannot be said that the composite of existing parts may be conceptual, having no realization externally, like the composite of stone and man, and of earth and sky. For we say: what is meant is that it is not one existent that subsists through an existence other than the existences of the parts. Otherwise, they have stated that the composite existing externally may have no reality distinct from the reality of its units, like ten men, and may be either with a prohibited form like the plant from elements, or without it, adding only a social configuration like the bed from pieces of wood. And if the totality is a contingent existent, then its cause is independent. [Its cause is] either itself—and that is clearly impossible—or a part of it, which is also impossible because it necessitates that part being a cause of itself and of its causes, since there is no meaning to causing the totality except causing the parts that constitute it, and no meaning to the independence of the cause except its being independent of what is other than it—or something external to it, which necessarily causes some of the parts, and the chain of effects terminates in it because the cause external to all contingents is Necessary in essence, and that part is not an effect of any part of the totality due to the impossibility of two independent causes concurring on one effect. For the discussion concerns the independent agent of existence. So a contradiction follows from two aspects: because it is assumed that the chain is unbroken and that every part of it is an effect of another part. And by what we have mentioned of the exposition, the refutation of the proof is repelled in detail: that if by 'the cause without which the totality of the chain cannot be' is meant the complete cause, then we do not concede the impossibility of it being the chain itself; it is only impossible if its priority is necessary. And it has preceded that the complete cause of a composite does not necessarily, nay must not, be prior to it, since among its parts are the parts that are the very effect. For if it is said: then it would follow that it is Necessary because its existence is from itself, and this alone suffices as impossibility. We say: it is denied; it would only follow if it did not depend on its part which is not its own essence, whether it is called 'other than it' or not. And if by 'efficient cause' is meant, then we do not concede the impossibility of it being some parts of the chain; it is only impossible if it necessitates it being a cause of every part of the effect, including itself and its causes. And this is denied, because it is possible that some parts of the composite effect are dependent on other than its agent, like the wood of the bed. We concede that. But we do not concede that the chain would be Necessary, because it is possible that there exist infinite chains of causes of a chain that the external from ... and effects infinite, and each of them depends on a cause external to it included in another without terminating at the Necessary. And even if it is conceded, the termination at the Necessary would follow, so the invalidity of regress does not follow, because it is possible that the totality of infinite causes and effects is a contingent existent dependent on the Necessary. And in summary: that it is refuted by the totality which is an expression of the Necessary and all existing possibles; for its cause is not itself, nor a part of it, for what was mentioned, nor external to it, because that would necessitate, along with the multiplicity of the Necessary, the effectness of the Necessary and the concurrence of agents if it is a cause of every part of the totality, and one of the two things if it is a cause of some parts. And the way of repulsion is that we have stated that what is meant by 'cause' is the independent efficient agent of existence, and we took the totality as the very collection of all possibles such that every part of it is an effect of a part, so the external to it would be nothing but the Necessary. And the least that follows from its independence in causality is that there exists in the totality a part that is not an effect of another part but specifically of the external, and that is the meaning of termination. And it is impossible for the independent in causality to be a part of the totality, because it would necessitate it being a cause of itself and of its causes, in realization of the meaning of independence. For if the cause of some parts were something else, the attainment of the totality would also depend on it, so neither of them would be independent. And this is unlike the composite composed of the Necessary...
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation by Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani of an objection concerning the impossibility of infinite regress and the necessity of an external cause for a series of contingent beings. The argument unfolds in several stages.

The author begins by clarifying the notion of a composite (مركب) of existing parts: it does not include the non-existent or the Necessary Being. He then addresses a potential objection that a composite may be merely conceptual (اعتباري) with no external reality, like a stone-man or earth-sky composite. The response is that what is meant here is that the composite is not a single existent with an existence over and above the existences of its parts. Externally existing composites may either have no reality distinct from their units (e.g., ten men) or have either a prohibited form (e.g., plant from elements) or a merely configurational unity (e.g., a bed from pieces of wood). If the totality (الجملة) is a contingent existent, it must have an independent cause (موجد بالاستقلال). This cause cannot be the totality itself (that would be circular), nor a part of it (that would make the part a cause of itself and of its causes, which is impossible), so it must be something external. That external cause, being the cause of some parts, terminates the chain of effects, because it is the Necessary in essence (واجب بالذات) and the part it causes is not caused by any other part of the totality (to avoid two independent causes concurring on one effect).

The discussion then moves to a detailed refutation of an attempted counterargument. It is said that if by 'cause of the whole chain' we mean the 'complete cause' (علة تامة), then it is not impossible for the chain itself to be its own complete cause, since the complete cause of a composite need not precede it (its parts are the very effect). The author rejects this: the complete cause's non-priority is allowed, but the issue is the regress of efficient causes. If by 'cause' we mean the 'efficient cause' (علة فاعلية), then it is not impossible for a part of the chain to act as the efficient cause of some other parts, as long as that part does not cause itself or its own causes. However, even if we concede that, the chain as a whole cannot be Necessary (واجب) because there could be infinite series of causes without terminating at the Necessary. But then the objection that the totality of all such series is contingent and depends on the Necessary Being still holds. The author then addresses a potential counterexample: the composite of the Necessary Being and all possibles. Its cause cannot be itself, nor a part (the Necessary is not a part), nor external (that would imply a second Necessary). The repulsion is that in our argument, 'cause' means the independent efficient agent, and we considered the totality of all possibles where every part is caused by another part, so the external to the series is the Necessary. The non-existence of an external cause would mean that some part of the series is itself independent, which is impossible because it would have to cause itself and its causes. Finally, the author distinguishes this from the composite of the Necessary and possibles, implying that the Necessary is not part of the chain of effects.

Key Terms
العلة التامة (al-ʿilla al-tāmma)
العلة الفاعلية (al-ʿilla al-fāʿiliyya)
السلسلة (al-silsila)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)
الواجب بالذات (al-wājib bi-l-dhāt)
الممكن (al-mumkin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An infinite regress of causes does not require a first cause; the series is self-sufficient.
Correction: The author shows that even an infinite series, considered as a totality, is contingent and depends on an external Necessary Being.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani, why does the possibility of infinite chains not refute the need for an external cause?
A) Because infinite chains are impossible.
B) Because the totality of infinite chains is contingent.
C) Because each chain has its own cause.
D) Because the Necessary Being is one of the chains.

Key Takeaway
An infinite regress of causes, considered as a whole, is still contingent and requires an external Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an unexpected hardship, do you instinctively search for a natural explanation or do you pause to recognize that the entire chain of events—and your very existence—depends moment by moment on the Necessary Being? How would living with that awareness change your immediate response to difficulty?
?
Consider the excuses you make for your own faults—an infinite chain of justifications that shifts blame to circumstances or others. Do you see the parallel with the argument that an infinite regress cannot escape dependence on a First Cause? What would it mean for you to stop the regress and admit your own contingency before God?
?
Taftazani argues that even if causes were infinite, the whole chain is still contingent. Reflect on your life: do you act as if your achievements are self-made, part of an endless chain of your own efforts? How could acknowledging that your entire existence is sustained by an external Cause transform your sense of gratitude and humility?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
8 min
Refutation of an Objection on the Necessity of the Causal Chain
The author refutes an objection that an infinite chain of causes would be necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author systematically refutes the objection that an infinite causal chain would force each cause to be necessary (wajib), and why the independent cause (al-fāʿil al-mustaqill) must be external to the chain.

Core Idea
The independent cause cannot be part of the causal chain without causing itself.

In this passage, the author presents a classic kalam objection and response concerning causal chains. The objector argues that if an infinite chain of causes were possible, each member would be necessary by itself, which is absurd. The author responds step by step, distinguishing between senses of 'cause' and introducing the concept of the totality (al-jumla) to show that the independent cause must be external. This lesson unpacks the argument and its key distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Carefully read the opening objection: 'If it is said: Then it would be necessary for it to be necessary...' Understand what the objector claims will happen if an infinite chain is allowed.
The objector thinks each cause would be wājib, which is impossible.
2
Follow the initial denial
The author immediately denies the premise. Notice how he says the necessity only follows if the cause does not depend on a part other than its essence. This is a key distinction: dependence on parts preserves contingency.
The author shifts the ground from necessity to dependency.
3
Distinguish the efficient cause
The author singles out the efficient cause (al-ʿilla al-fāʿiliyya). He argues that it is not impossible for the efficient cause to be part of the chain as long as it does not cause every part of the effect including itself.
This limits the scope of the objection.
4
Consider infinite chains without termination
The author then suggests it is possible to have infinite chains of causes and effects that do not terminate at the Necessary Being. Each member depends on another, but the totality as a whole could be possible (mumkin) and depend on the Necessary.
This undercuts the necessity for each link to be wājib.
5
Grasp the totality argument and the bed analogy
The culmination: the independent cause of the totality cannot be part of the totality (or else self-causation). The author uses the example of a bed to show that the independent agent may be composite (carpenter + wood), but for the totality of all possibles, the independent cause must be external and unique.
This refutes the objection by showing that the chain can be infinite while grounded in the Necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فيلزم أن يكون واجباً لكون وجودها من ذاتها وكفى بهـذا(١) استحالة قلنا ممنوع وإنما يلزم لو لم يفتقر (٢) إلى جزئها الذي ليس نفس ذاتها سواء سمى غيرها، غيرها، أو لم يسم وإن أريد العلة الفاعلية فلا نسلم إستحالة كونها بعض أجزاء السلسلة وإنما يستحيل لو لزم كونها علة لكل جزء من أجزاء المعلول حتى نفسه وعلله، وهو ممنوع لجواز أن يكون بعض أجزاء المعلول المركب مستنداً إلى غير فاعله كالخشب من السرير سلمنا ذلك. لكن لا نسلم السلسلة يكون واجباً لجواز أن توجد سلاسل غير متناهية من علل سلسلة أن الخارج من و معلولات غير متناهية وكل منها يستند إلى علة خارجة عنها داخلة في أخرى من غير انتهاء إلى الواجب . ولو سلم (٣) لزم الانتهاء إلى الواجب، فلا يلزم بطلان التسلسل لجواز أن يكون مجموع العلل والمعلولات الغير المتناهية موجوداً ممكناً (٤) مستنداً إلى الواجب، وإجمالاً بأنه منقوض بالجملة التي هي عبارة (٥) عن الواجب وجميع الممكنات الموجودة فإن علتها ليست نفسها، ولا جزءاً منها لما ذكر، ولا خارجاً عنها لاستلزامه مع تعدد الواجب معلولية الواجب واجتماع المؤثرين إن كان علة لكل جزء من أجزاء الجملة، وأحد الأمرين إن كان علة لبعض الأجزاء ووجه الاندفاع أنا قد (٦) صرحنا بأن المراد بالعلة الفاعل المستقل بالإيجاد وأخذنا الجملة نفس جميع الممكنات بحيث يكون كل جزء منها معلولاً لجزء () ، فلم يكن الخارج عنها إلا واجباً، وأقل ما لزم من استقلاله بالعلية (۸) أن يوجد في الجملة جزء لا يكون معلولاً لجزء آخر بل للخارج خاصة، وهو معنى الانقطاع ولم يمكن أن يكون المستقل بالعلية جزءاً من الجملة للزوم كونه علة لنفسه ولعلله تحقيقاً بمعنى الاستقلال، إذ لو كان الموجد لبعض الأجزاء شيئاً آخر لتوقف حصول الجملة عليه أيضاً، فلم يكن أحدهما مستقلاً، وهذا بخلاف المجموع المركب من الواجب والممكنات . فإنه جاز أن يستقل بإيجاده بعض أجزائه الذي هو موجود بذاته مستغن عن غيره، وأمات السرير ففاعله المستقل ليس هو النجار وحده، بل مع فاعل الخشبات
If it is said: Then it would be necessary for it to be necessary (wājib) because its existence is from its essence, and this alone is impossible. We say: It is denied. It would follow only if it did not depend on a part of it that is not its own essence, whether that part is called 'other' or not. And if what is meant is the efficient cause (al-ʿilla al-fāʿiliyya), then we do not accept the impossibility of it being some parts of the chain. It would be impossible only if it were a cause of every part of the effect, including itself and its causes, which is denied because it is possible that some parts of a composite effect are attributed to other than its agent, like the wood of a bed. We grant that. But we do not accept that the chain would be necessary (wājib) because it is possible that there exist infinite chains of causes, such that what comes out of them and their effects are infinite, each depending on a cause external to it but internal to another, without ending at the Necessary (al-wājib). And even if we grant that, the ending at the Necessary would follow, so the invalidity of the chain does not follow because it is possible that the totality of infinite causes and effects exists as a possible thing (mumkin) depending on the Necessary. In summary: it is refuted by the totality (al-jumla) which is composed of the Necessary and all existing possibles, for its cause is not itself, nor a part of it for what was mentioned, nor external to it because that would entail, given the multiplicity of the Necessary, the effecthood of the Necessary and the concurrence of agents if it is a cause of every part of the totality, or one of the two things if it is a cause of some parts. The way to rebut is that we have stated that what is meant by the cause is the independent agent in bringing into existence (al-fāʿil al-mustaqill bi-l-ījād), and we took the totality as the sum of all possibles such that every part of it is an effect of a part, so nothing external to it is except the Necessary. The least that follows from its independence in causality is that there exists in the totality a part that is not an effect of another part but specifically of the external, which is the meaning of discontinuity (al-inqiṭāʿ). And it is impossible for the independent cause to be a part of the totality because that would entail it being a cause of itself and its causes, fulfilling the meaning of independence. For if the bringer into existence of some parts were something else, then the attainment of the totality would depend on that other as well, so neither would be independent. This is unlike the composite aggregate of the Necessary and possibles: for it is possible that some of its parts that exist by themselves and are independent of others are independent in bringing it into existence. As for the bed, its independent agent is not the carpenter alone but rather together with the agent of the wood.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents a kalam-style objection-and-answer (قيل/قلنا) concerning causal chains. The objector argues that if a chain of causes were possible, each member would be necessary (واجب) by itself, which is absurd. The author (the commentator, likely al-Taftazani or the original author) responds in several steps: (1) He denies the premise that a cause must be necessary; rather, it depends on a part that is not its essence. (2) He distinguishes between the efficient cause (العلة الفاعلية) and other senses; regarding the efficient cause, it is not impossible for it to be part of the chain as long as it does not cause every part of the effect including itself. (3) He then considers the possibility of infinite causal chains without termination at the Necessary (الواجب). The response uses the concept of 'the totality' (الجملة) comprising the Necessary and all possibles. The key point: the independent cause (الفاعل المستقل) that brings the totality into existence cannot be part of that totality, because that would require it to be a cause of itself and its own causes. However, a composite of the Necessary and possibles can have an independent cause that is itself a part of that composite (like the Necessary being the cause of possibles while Himself uncaused). The example of the bed illustrates that the independent cause of a composite may itself be composite (carpenter + wood). The author refutes the objection by showing that the independent cause of the entire chain must be external and unique, thus upholding the possibility of an infinite chain of effects grounded in the Necessary.

Key Terms
واجب
علة فاعلية
فاعل مستقل
تسلسل
جملة
انقطاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that an infinite causal chain would force each link to be necessary (wajib) by itself, making the chain impossible.
Correction: The author shows that each link can be contingent (mumkin) by depending on another cause, and the entire infinite chain can be a contingent whole depending on the Necessary. The independent cause of the chain cannot be part of the chain itself.

Checkpoint
Why cannot the independent cause (الفاعل المستقل) be a part of the totality (الجملة) according to the author?
A) Because it would then cause itself and its own causes.
B) Because the totality must be finite.
C) Because the independent cause must be a composite of many agents.
D) Because the chain would then be necessary.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the objection by showing that an infinite causal chain is possible as a contingent whole grounded in the Necessary, and the independent cause must be external to the chain to avoid self-causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience success or comfort, do you unconsciously credit secondary causes (like your effort or luck) as if they were independent? How does this lesson's emphasis on the single independent cause (God) call you to redirect your gratitude? Will you pause right now to thank Him specifically for one recent blessing, recognizing that every link in the chain is dependent on Him?
?
In times of difficulty, do you trace your problems back to immediate causes (people, circumstances) and forget that the entire chain is contingent on the Necessary? This lesson shows that infinite chains are possible but all depend on God. How can you train yourself, starting today, to see every event – even hardships – as directly tied to His wisdom and will? What practical reminder can you set for yourself?
?
This passage argues that the independent cause cannot be part of the causal chain without absurdity. Reflect: Is God truly the ultimate independent cause in your daily thoughts and actions, or do you sometimes treat Him as one link among many? What step of reliance can you take today that acknowledges His absolute independence and your complete dependence?

Objection to the Independent Cause Premise for Composites
Objection to the independent cause premise for composite parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to the premise that an independent cause must cause every part of a composite individually, and how the second interpretation allows for sequential causation without logical absurdity.

Core Idea
An independent cause can cause a composite sequentially through its parts without contradiction.

This passage presents a critical objection to a key premise used earlier in the argument about causal chains. The author examines two interpretations of the premise that the independent cause of a composite is a cause of every part, showing that the second interpretation avoids the absurdities of direct simultaneous causation and does not prove that the independent cause cannot be part of the chain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Notice the structure: an objection (اعتراض) with two interpretations (إما أن يراد... وإما أن يراد).
The passage is dense; focus on the flow of the objection.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Locate where the author says 'either it is meant that...' and 'Or it is meant that...'. Understand what each interpretation claims about the relationship between the independent cause and the parts of the composite.
The first interpretation leads to absurdity; the second attempts to avoid it.
3
Understand the critique of the second interpretation
The author argues that even if the second interpretation avoids immediate absurdity, it fails to prove that the independent cause cannot be part of the chain. Note the mention of 'aggregate of parts' and 'pure effect'.
This is the key move: the objection does not disprove the original argument, but it shows the premise is not necessarily problematic.
4
Reflect on the implication for the overall argument
Consider how this objection relates to the broader discussion about causality and divine existence. Does it weaken or strengthen the previous reasoning?
The passage shows that the earlier premise is not obviously false, but the objection itself has limits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم قد (١) يرد على المقدمة القائلة بأن العلة المستقلة للمركب من الأجزاء الممكنة علة (٢) لكل جزء منه اعتراض وهو أنه إما أن يراد أنها بنفسها علة مستقلة لكل جزء حتى يكون علة هذا الجزء هي بعينها علة ذلك الجزء، وهذا باطل لأن المركب قد يكون بحيث تحدث أجزاؤه شيئاً فشيئاً كخشبات السرير وهيئته الاجتماعية، فعند حدوث الجزء الأول. إن لم توجد العلة المستقلة التي فرضناها علة لكل جزء لزم تقدم المعلول على علته، وهو ظاهر. وإن وجدت لزم تخلف المعلول أعني الجزء الآخر من علته المستقلة ،بالإيجاد وقد مر بطلانه وإما أن يراد أنها علة لكل جزء من المركب، إما بنفسها أو بجزء منها بحيث يكون كل جزء معلولاً لها أو لجزء منها من غير افتقار إلى أمر خارج عنها، وإذا كان المعلول المركب مترتب الأجزاء كانت علتها المستقلة أيضاً مترتبة الأجزاء يحدث كل جزء منه لكل (٣) جزء منها يقارنه بحسب الزمان، ولا يلزم التقدم ولا التخلف ، () وهذا أيضاً(ه) فاسد من جهة أنه لا يفيد المطلوب (1) أعني امتناع كون العلة المستقلة للسلسلة جزءاً منها إذ من (۷) أجزائها ما يجوز أن يكون علة بهذا المعنى من غير أن يلزم علية الشيء لنفسه أو لعلله ، وذلك مجموع الأجزاء التي كل منها معروض للعلية والمعلولية بحيث لا يخرج عنها إلا المعلول المحض (۸) المتأخر عن الكل بحسب العلية المتقدم عليها بحسب الرتبة حيث يعتبر من الجانب المتناهي، ولذا يعبر عن المجموع تارة بما قبل المعلول الأخير، وتارة بما بعد المعلول الأول. . ففي السلسلة تتحقق السلسلة عند تحققها، ويقع بكل جزء منها الجملة هي جزء من جزء منها ولا يلزم من عليتها للسلسلة تقدم الشيء على نفسه .
Yes, there is an objection to the premise that the independent cause (al-ʿilla al-mustaqilla) of a composite of possible parts is a cause (ʿilla) of every part of it. It is that either it is meant that it is by itself an independent cause of every part, such that the cause of this part is exactly the cause of that part—and this is false because a composite may be such that its parts come into existence gradually, like the wooden planks of a bed and their collective configuration. At the occurrence of the first part, if the independent cause that we supposed to be a cause of every part did not exist, then the effect would precede its cause, which is obvious. And if it did exist, then the other part would lag behind its independent cause in bringing into existence, and its invalidity has been mentioned. Or it is meant that it is a cause of every part of the composite, either by itself or by a part of it, such that every part is an effect of it or of a part of it without needing anything external. And when the composite effect is arranged in parts, its independent cause is also arranged in parts; each part of the effect occurs for each part of the cause, correlating in time, and neither precedence nor lagging follows. This also is invalid because it does not achieve the desired point, namely the impossibility of the independent cause of the chain being a part of it, since among its parts there is what can be a cause in this sense without entailing a thing being a cause of itself or its causes. That is the aggregate of parts, each of which is subject to causality and effecthood, such that nothing exits from it except the pure effect (al-maʿlūl al-maḥḍ) which is posterior to the whole in terms of causality and prior to it in terms of rank, considered from the finite side. Therefore, the aggregate is sometimes expressed as 'what is before the last effect' and sometimes as 'what is after the first effect'. Thus, in the chain, the chain is realized upon its realization, and for each part of it, the totality is a part of a part of it, and from its causality of the chain, the precedence of a thing over itself does not follow.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents a further objection (اعتراض) to a premise that was used in the earlier argument, namely that the independent cause (العلة المستقلة) of a composite of possible parts must be a cause of every part. The objection examines two possible interpretations of this premise. First, if it means that the same independent cause directly causes every part individually, then for a composite whose parts arise sequentially (like the planks of a bed and their arrangement), this would entail either that the cause does not exist when the first part arises (leading to effect preceding cause) or that the later part is delayed relative to the cause (which is impossible). Second, if it means that the cause causes every part either directly or through its own parts, then for a sequential composite, the independent cause can also be sequential, each part of the cause producing a corresponding part of the effect in time. This avoids precedence and lag, but it does not prove that the independent cause cannot be part of the chain. In fact, it allows that within the chain there can be an aggregate of parts that are both cause and effect with respect to each other, such that the only pure effect (المعلول المحض) is the final one. In such a chain, the independent cause of the chain can be a part of the chain without circularity. The author thus shows that the premise about the independent cause being a cause of every part does not lead to impossibility, and the earlier argument against the chain being part of the totality is not undermined.

Key Terms
علة مستقلة
معلول محض
تقدم
تخلف
مركب
اعتراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that an independent cause must directly cause every part instantaneously, but this passage shows that sequential causation through parts is possible without logical absurdity.
Correction: The independent cause can cause parts sequentially via its own parts, as long as each part of the effect corresponds to a part of the cause in time.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the objection against the premise that the independent cause is a cause of every part?
A) That the premise is true and leads to circularity.
B) That the premise allows for sequential causation, thus not proving impossibility.
C) That the independent cause must be external to the chain.
D) That the premise only applies to finite composites.

Key Takeaway
The objection demonstrates that the premise does not automatically lead to impossibility; sequential causation through parts is logically coherent.

?
When you pray for something to happen, do you expect God to cause it instantly and directly, or do you accept that He might work through gradual, hidden means? Which interpretation from this lesson do you lean toward, and how does that affect your patience?

Objection to the Independent Cause Premise
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes an objection to the premise about the independent cause of a composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to the premise that the independent cause of a composite is a cause for each part, and how Al-Taftazani refutes it by analyzing two interpretations.

Core Idea
An objection to causality in composites is refuted by distinguishing two meanings of an independent cause.

This passage tackles a precise objection to a causal premise. The objector argues that if the independent cause of a composite is a cause for each part, it leads to absurdities. Al-Taftazani carefully dissects the objection by clarifying what the premise could mean, showing that under either interpretation, the objection fails. This discussion reveals deeper insights into causality and the nature of series.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage once to understand the objection and the premise it targets.
Notice the example of the bed and its parts.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Al-Taftazani breaks the objection into two possible meanings of the premise. Find and label them.
The first: cause is exactly the same for all parts. The second: cause is a cause for each part by itself or by a part of itself.
3
Analyze the refutation of the first interpretation
Understand why the first reading leads to temporal absurdity with ordered parts.
Either the cause is absent when the first part arises (effect precedes cause) or present but later parts lag behind (effect lags).
4
Understand the second interpretation and conclusion
See how the objector's alternative fails to prove its point, and how the series example shows that a part of the series can be a cause without circularity.
The 'pure effect' concept is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والممكنات . فإنه جاز أن يستقل بإيجاده بعض أجزائه الذي هو موجود بذاته مستغن عن غيره، وأما السرير ففاعله المستقل ليس هو النجار وحده، بل مع فاعل الخشبات نعم قد يرد على المقدمة القائلة بأن العلة المستقلة للمركب من الأجزاء الممكنة علة لكل جزء منه اعتراض وهو أنه إما أن يراد أنها بنفسها علة مستقلة لكل جزء حتى تكون علة هذا الجزء هي بعينها علة ذلك الجزء، وهذا باطل لأن المركب قد يكون بحيث تحدث أجزاؤه شيئاً فشيئاً كخشبات السرير وهيئته الاجتماعية، فعند حدوث الجزء الأول. إن لم توجد العلة المستقلة التي فرضناها علة لكل جزء لزم تقدم المعلول على علته، وهو ظاهر. وإن وجدت لزم تخلف المعلول أعني الجزء الآخر من علته المستقلة بالإيجاد وقد مر بطلانه وإما أن يراد أنها علة لكل جزء من المركب، إما بنفسها أو بجزء منها بحيث يكون كل جزء معلولاً لها أو لجزء منها من غير افتقار إلى أمر خارج عنها، وإذا كان المعلول المركب مترتب الأجزاء كانت علتها المستقلة أيضاً مترتبة الأجزاء يحدث كل جزء منه لكل جزء منها يقارنه بحسب الزمان، ولا يلزم التقدم ولا التخلف، وهذا أيضاً فاسد من جهة أنه لا يفيد المطلوب أعني امتناع كون العلة المستقلة للسلسلة جزءاً منها إذ من أجزائها ما يجوز أن يكون علة بهذا المعنى من غير أن يلزم علية الشيء لنفسه أو لعلله، وذلك مجموع الأجزاء التي كل منها معروض للعلية والمعلولية بحيث لا يخرج عنها إلا المعلول المحض المتأخر عن الكل بحسب العلية المتقدم عليها بحسب الرتبة حيث يعتبر من الجانب المتناهي، ولذا يعبر عن المجموع تارة بما قبل المعلول الأخير، وتارة بما بعد المعلول الأول. ففي السلسلة تتحقق السلسلة عند تحققها، ويقع بكل جزء منها الجملة هي جزء من جزء منها ولا يلزم من عليتها للسلسلة تقدم الشيء على نفسه .
And the possible things. For it is possible that some of its parts, which exist by themselves and are independent of others, are independent in bringing it into existence. As for the bed, its independent agent is not the carpenter alone, but rather together with the agent of the planks. Yes, an objection may be raised against the premise that the independent cause of the composite of possible parts is a cause for each part: that it is either meant that it is by itself an independent cause for each part, so that the cause of this part is exactly the cause of that part, and this is false because the composite may be such that its parts arise little by little, like the planks of the bed and their social configuration. For when the first part arises, if the independent cause that we assumed to be a cause for each part does not exist, then the effect would precede its cause, which is evident; and if it does exist, then the effect, i.e., the other part, would lag behind its independent cause in bringing into existence, and its falsity has already been discussed. Or it is meant that it is a cause for each part of the composite, either by itself or by a part of it, such that each part is an effect of it or of a part of it without needing anything external. And if the composite effect has ordered parts, then its independent cause also has ordered parts, each part of it occurs for each part of it concurrent in time, and neither precedence nor lagging follows. This too is invalid because it does not achieve the desired goal, namely, the impossibility of the independent cause of the series being part of it, since among its parts there may be that which can be a cause in this sense without necessitating the causality of the thing for itself or for its causes. That is the totality of the parts each of which is subject to causality and effecthood, such that nothing exits from it except the pure effect, which is posterior to the whole in terms of causality and prior to it in terms of rank, considering it from the finite side. Hence the totality is sometimes expressed as 'that which is before the last effect' and sometimes as 'that which is after the first effect.' Thus in the series, the series is realized upon its realization, and by each part of it, the whole is a part of a part of it, and its causality for the series does not necessitate the thing's priority over itself.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes an objection to a key premise: that the independent cause of a composite of possible parts is a cause for each part. The objector argues that this leads to absurdity. Al-Taftazani first clarifies the objector's two possible interpretations: (1) the cause is exactly the same cause for each part, and (2) the cause is a cause for each part either by itself or by a part of itself. For the first interpretation, he shows that with temporally ordered parts, either the cause would be absent when the first part arises (leading to the effect preceding its cause) or present for all parts (but then later parts would lag behind their cause). Both are false. For the second interpretation, he argues that even if the cause has ordered parts concurrent with the composite's parts, this still does not prove that the cause cannot be part of the series; indeed, a part of the series could be a cause in this way without circularity. He then introduces the concept of the 'pure effect' and the totality of parts that are both cause and effect, concluding that the independent cause of the series being a part of it does not entail self-priority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The independent cause of a series cannot be part of the series itself, because that would be circular.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows that if the cause is understood as a cause for each part by itself or by a part of itself, it can be part of the series without circularity, as demonstrated by the concept of pure effect and the totality of parts.

Checkpoint

A) That the cause is the same for all parts; rejected because it leads to temporal absurdity with ordered parts.
B) That the cause is a part of itself; rejected because it leads to circularity.
C) That the cause is external; rejected because it would require an infinite regress.
D) That the cause is multiple; rejected because it contradicts unity.

Key Takeaway
The objection that the independent cause of a composite cannot be a cause for each part is refuted by clarifying that the premise can be understood in two ways, neither of which leads to absurdity; moreover, a part of a causal series can serve as an independent cause without circularity.

Answering the Objection of Infinite Regress in Causality
Al-Taftazani answers the objection that the aggregate cause itself is possible and requires a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Taftazani answers the objection that the aggregate cause itself requires a cause, by appealing to an infinite regress of aggregates.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is acceptable when each aggregate's cause is the previous aggregate in the series.

In this short passage, Al-Taftazani addresses a potential objection to his causal argument. The objector claims that if the cause of a series is an aggregate of parts, that aggregate itself is contingent and needs a cause, leading to an infinite regress. Al-Taftazani's answer is that the cause of each aggregate is the preceding aggregate, and this regress continues without end. Understanding this objection and its answer clarifies the role of infinite regress in kalam arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part of the passage: the objector claims the aggregate cause is possible and needs a cause.
2
Follow the answer
Read Al-Taftazani's response: the cause of that aggregate is the previous aggregate containing the last effect, and so on.
3
Reflect on infinite regress
Consider why this infinite regress is not problematic in this context—it is a regress of causes for aggregates, not a vicious circle.
4
Relate to the broader argument
Think about how this answer defends the causal principle against the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : المجموع الذي هو العلة أيضاً ممكن محتاج إلى علة أجيب : بأن علته المجموع الذي قيل ما فيه من المعلول الأخير، وهكذا في كل مجموع قبله لا إلى نهاية .
If it is said: The aggregate that is the cause is also possible, needing a cause. It is answered: Its cause is the aggregate that is said to contain the last effect, and likewise for every aggregate before it, without end.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This is a short objection-and-answer pair. The objector argues that if the cause of the series is an aggregate of parts, that aggregate itself is possible and thus requires a cause, leading to infinite regress. Al-Taftazani answers by identifying the cause of that aggregate as the aggregate that contains the last effect—i.e., the aggregate one step earlier in the series. This continues indefinitely, but the regress is not vicious because it is a regress of causes for aggregates, not a circular or self-defeating chain. The answer implies that such an infinite regress is acceptable in this context.

معلول أخير
لا إلى نهاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite regress is always invalid or vicious.
Correction: Infinite regress can be acceptable if it does not lead to contradiction or circularity. Here, the regress of aggregates causes is linear and well-defined, so it is a valid explanation.

Checkpoint

A) The aggregate is necessary, not possible.
B) The cause of the aggregate is the aggregate containing the last effect, and this continues without end.
C) The aggregate is its own cause, ending the regress.
D) The objection is dismissed as irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani defends the causal chain by accepting an infinite regress of aggregates, each caused by the previous one.

Objection: The Pure Effect Cannot Be an Independent Cause
Al-Taftazani defends that the aggregate including the pure effect is still an independent cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Taftazani argues that the pure effect's assistance does not undermine the independence of the cause, because independence is defined in relation to external causes only.

Core Idea
Independence from external cause does not require independence from internal components.

This lesson presents a key objection to the theory of causality in a series of contingent beings: if the last pure effect contributes to the existence of the series, how can the aggregate be an independent cause? Al-Taftazani clarifies that independence is about needing an external cause, not about needing internal constituents.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection as presented in the passage. Note the two reasons given: (1) the aggregate itself is possible and needs a cause, (2) the pure effect must assist.
2
Identify Al-Taftazani's response
His response has two parts: first, a definition of independence; second, an application of that definition to show that the pure effect does not require an external cause.
3
Understand the key distinction
Notice the distinction between external and internal assistance. Independence only requires no external cause; internal causes are acceptable.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this objection and answer are important for understanding causality in the series of contingent beings.
This clarifies how the last effect can be part of the causal chain without requiring an external cause for the aggregate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ما بعد المعلول المحض لا يصلح علة مستقلة بإيجاد السلسلة لأنه ممكن يحتاج إلى علته وهكذا كل مجموع يفرض، فلا توجد السلسلة إلا تلك العلل، ولأنه ليس بكاف في تحقق السلسلة، بل لا بد من بمعاونة من المعلول المحض أيضاً. قلنا هذا لا يقدح في الاستقلال لأن معناه عدم الافتقار في الإيجاد إلى معاونة علة خارجة، وقد فرضنا أن علة كل مجموع أمر داخل فيه لا خارج عنه وظاهر أنه لا داخل لمعلوله الأخير في إيجاده .
If it is said: That which is after the pure effect is not suitable as an independent cause for bringing the series into existence, because it is possible and needs its cause, and likewise every aggregate that is assumed; thus the series exists only through those causes; and because it is not sufficient for the realization of the series, but rather the pure effect must also assist. We say: This does not harm independence, because its meaning is the lack of need in bringing into existence for the assistance of an external cause. And we have assumed that the cause of every aggregate is something internal to it, not external to it. And it is evident that there is no internal [cause] for its last effect in bringing it into existence.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This is another objection and answer. The objector claims that the aggregate after the pure effect (i.e., the aggregate that excludes the last effect) cannot be an independent cause because it itself is possible and needs a cause. Moreover, the series would not exist without the pure effect's cooperation. Al-Taftazani responds that independence means not needing an external cause; the cause of each aggregate is internal to that aggregate. The pure effect, being the last part of the series, is internal to the aggregate that includes it, but the cause of that aggregate is something internal (the earlier parts), so the pure effect does not need an external cause. Thus, the objection does not undermine independence.

إيجاد

داخل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the pure effect assists, the cause is not truly independent.
Correction: Al-Taftazani clarifies that assistance from within does not negate independence; independence only requires no external cause.

Checkpoint
Why does the answer say the pure effect's assistance does not harm independence?
A) Because the pure effect is a necessary being.
B) Because independence means not needing an external cause, and the pure effect is internal.
C) Because the pure effect is not a cause at all.
D) Because the series does not actually need the pure effect.

Key Takeaway
The pure effect's assistance does not undermine the cause's independence because independence is defined by the absence of need for an external cause, and the pure effect is internal to the aggregate.

Objection on Infinite Series and Al-Taftazani's Response
Al-Taftazani addresses an objection about infinite series lacking a last effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Taftazani's response to the objection that infinite series lack a last effect, and how he preserves causal order through sub-chains.

Core Idea
Even infinite series can have ordered sub-chains with 'last effects'.

After establishing the argument against infinite regress, Al-Taftazani anticipates an objection: what if the entire series—whether one chain or multiple—is taken as a whole? The objector claims that in an infinite series there is no last effect, so the earlier prohibition fails. Al-Taftazani's reply is terse but significant: he insists that even infinite aggregates can be analyzed into parts that have their own last effects, thereby maintaining the causal structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage which presents the objection against the prohibition of infinite regress.
Notice the phrase 'فهذا المنع أيضاً مندفع' — the objector thinks the earlier argument is repelled.
2
Identify the objector's reasoning
The objector says 'إذ ليس هناك معلول أخير' (since there is no last effect) and 'ومجموع مرتب قبله' (and an ordered aggregate before it). Understand why this challenges the earlier argument.
The earlier argument required a last effect for the aggregate to be defined.
3
Analyze Al-Taftazani's response
Read his reply: 'بل وأراد بأن يجعل علتها الجزء الذي هو المجموعات الغير المتناهية التي قبل كان معلولاتها الأخيرة لغير المتناهية'. He says the cause can be the part that consists of the infinite aggregates before, whose last effects are also infinite.
The response implies that even within infinite series, each sub-aggregate has its own 'last effect' relative to it.
4
Reflect on the preservation of order
Consider how this response allows causal order to be maintained even in an infinite regress, by focusing on parts rather than the whole.
Al-Taftazani does not abandon the argument but reinterprets the structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إذا أخذت الجملة أعني من أن تكون سلسلة واحدة أو سلاسل غير متناهية على ما ذكرتم، فهذا المنع أيضاً مندفع ، إذ ليس هناك معلول أخير، ومجموع مرتب قبله . قلنا : بل وأراد بأن يجعل علتها الجزء الذي هو المجموعات الغير المتناهية التي قبل كان معلولاتها الأخيرة لغير المتناهية .
If it is said: If you take the whole, meaning it is either one series or infinite series as you mentioned, then this prohibition is also repelled, since there is no last effect, and an ordered aggregate before it. We say: Rather, [it is] and he intended to make its cause the part that is the infinite aggregates that before were their last effects for the infinite.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This objection argues that if the series is taken as a whole (either as one infinite series or as multiple infinite series), then the earlier argument fails because there is no last effect to define the aggregate. Al-Taftazani responds by suggesting that the cause can still be identified as the part consisting of the infinite aggregates before, whose 'last effects' are themselves infinite – that is, each sub-series has its own last effect in an infinite regress. The answer is terse but implies that even with infinite series, a similar structure of before and after can be maintained.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite series cannot have any 'last effect' at all, so the argument against infinite regress fails.
Correction: Al-Taftazani argues that even in infinite series, sub-chains can have their own last effects, maintaining causal order and the validity of the original argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you trace back the causes of your own existence, do you ever reach a point where you say 'this is the ultimate cause'? How does the idea of a 'last effect' in a sub-chain challenge you to think more deeply about the chain of events in your life?
?
Al-Taftazani argues that even in infinite series, we can find order and endpoints. In your own life, are there areas where you feel lost in an 'infinite' cycle of habits or thoughts? What would it mean for you to identify a 'last effect'—a point where you can say 'this stops here' and take responsibility?
?
The objection in this lesson suggests that without a clear endpoint, reasoning fails. Have you ever abandoned a line of inquiry because it seemed too complex or infinite? What would it look like to persist and find the 'ordered part' that brings clarity?

Objection: No Part Is More Entitled to Be Cause
Al-Taftazani defends the specificity of the part before the last effect as the cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that no part of a causal series can be privileged as the cause, and Al-Taftazani's initial response that the part before the last effect is specifically determined.

Core Idea
Not all parts are equal—the part before the last effect is the cause.

In this objection, Al-Taftazani faces a challenge to his causal theory: if all parts of a series are equal, then no part can be the cause. He begins his answer by asserting that one part—the one immediately preceding the final effect—is uniquely determined. Although the full argument is cut off, this passage introduces a key move in the debate about causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Pay attention to the two reasons given why no part can be the cause.
The objection presents two arguments: lack of priority, and the effect being more entitled.
2
Analyze the two arguments
Identify why each argument leads to the conclusion that no part can be the cause of the whole. The first is based on equality of parts, the second on the effect's greater influence.
3
Follow Al-Taftazani's rebuttal
Read his answer: he rejects the objection and asserts that the part before the last effect is specifically determined as the cause. Notice that the reasoning is cut off at 'because'.
The incomplete text invites speculation about how he would complete the argument.
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
Consider why this matters for causality in kalam. The discussion is about how a series of causes leads to a final effect, and which part is the true cause.

However, the text cuts off at 'because' (لأن), leaving the reasoning incomplete. Presumably the next page completes the argument, but within this window we see only the start of the response.
