The Totality's Causality and Infinite Regress
Answers the objection that the total cause is possible and requires a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that the totality (the cause) itself is possible and requires a cause is answered by appealing to an internal causal chain without end.

Core Idea
The cause of each totality is the previous totality, forming an endless internal chain.

This passage presents a classic objection in kalam: if the totality (the sum of causes) is itself possible, then it too needs a cause. The answer shows that the cause of any totality is simply the preceding totality in the series, which includes the last effect. This avoids needing an external cause while preserving causal continuity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: the totality which is the cause is itself possible and needs a cause.
2
Identify the answer
Notice how the answer responds: the cause of that totality is the previous totality containing the last effect.
3
Grasp the implication
Reflect on the chain continuing without end – this is the key to avoiding regress problems.
The cause is internal to the series, not external.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : المجموع الذي هو العلة أيضاً ممكن محتاج إلى علة أجيب : بأن علته المجموع الذي قيل ما فيه من المعلول الأخير، وهكذا في كل مجموع قبله لا إلى نهاية .
If it is said: The totality which is the cause is also possible, needing a cause. It is answered: Its cause is the totality that was mentioned, which contains the last effect, and so on for every totality before it without end.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer about infinite regress. The objector argues that the totality (al-majmu') functioning as a cause is itself possible (mumkin) and requires a cause, leading to regress. The answer explains that the cause of that totality is the previous totality including the last effect (al-ma'lul al-akhir), and this chain continues indefinitely. Crucially, the cause is internal to the series, not external, preserving the causal chain's coherence.

Key Terms
مجموع
علة
ممكن
معلول أخير
لا إلى نهاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer merely pushes the problem back without solving it.
Correction: The point is that the cause is internal to the series, so no external cause is needed. The chain is coherent because each totality's cause is part of the series itself.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what is the cause of each totality in the chain?
A) An external transcendent cause
B) The totality that contains the last effect
C) The individual parts alone
D) Nothing, it is self-caused

Key Takeaway
The cause of each totality is the previous totality in the endless chain, avoiding the need for an external cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does the answer from this passage redirect your attention to the internal chain of causes that Allah has created?
?
The passage argues that each totality's cause is the previous totality, forming an endless internal chain. In your own journey of seeking closeness to Allah, do you ever feel stuck in a regress of spiritual efforts without seeing the end? How does the concept of an endless internal chain (without external need) change your perspective on your current state?
?
What is one area of your life where you have been waiting for an external 'cause' to change your situation, instead of recognizing the internal chain of causes already at work? How can you act today based on this knowledge?

The Independence of the Final Effect in Causality
Objection and answer on whether a pure effect can be independent cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of 'independence' (istiqlal) in causal series and how the objection against the final effect being an independent cause is refuted.

Core Idea
Independence in causality means not needing an external cause, not acting alone.

This passage addresses a critical objection to the argument that a final effect can independently cause a series of effects. The objector claims that because the pure effect is itself contingent, it cannot bring about a whole series without external help. The response clarifies that independence in this context only means free from external assistance, and that each totality is caused by something internal to it, not by the final effect itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection part (فإن قيل ...) and note the two reasons: (1) the series would need infinite causes, (2) the pure effect alone is insufficient; it needs assistance from itself? Actually the objection says assistance from the pure effect is also necessary.
The objection is that the pure effect cannot be an independent cause because it is possible (contingent) and because the series would require external assistance.
2
Read the answer patiently
Read the answer (قلنا ...) and identify the clarification: independence means no need for external cause. The cause of each totality is internal, not external. The last effect does not contribute to its own causation.
The answer redefines independence and applies the internal cause principle.
3
Connect to the broader argument
Consider how this defense preserves the notion that the final effect can be an independent cause for the series, as long as its independence is understood correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ما بعد المعلول المحض لا يصلح علة مستقلة بإيجاد السلسلة لأنه ممكن يحتاج إلى علته وهكذا كل مجموع يفرض، فلا توجد السلسلة إلا تلك العلل، ولأنه ليس بكاف في تحقق السلسلة، بل لا بد من بمعاونة من المعلول المحض أيضاً. قلنا هذا لا يقدح في الاستقلال لأن معناه عدم الافتقار في الإيجاد إلى معاونة علة خارجة، وقد فرضنا أن علة كل مجموع أمر داخل فيه لا خارج عنه وظاهر أنه لا داخل لمعلوله الأخير في إيجاده .
If it is said: What is after the pure effect is not suitable as an independent cause for bringing the series into existence because it is possible needing its cause, and so is every totality you suppose, so the series only exists through those causes, and because it is not sufficient for the series' realization; rather, assistance from the pure effect is also necessary. We say: This does not impair independence because its meaning is not needing assistance from an external cause, and we have assumed that the cause of every totality is something internal to it, not external, and it is apparent that the effect's last element has no part in bringing it into existence.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection: that the pure effect (al-ma'lul al-mahd) cannot serve as an independent cause for the series because it itself is possible and insufficient without assistance from the pure effect. The answer clarifies that independence means not needing an external cause, and the cause of each totality is internal to that totality. The last effect does not contribute to its own causation, so the objection does not hold.

Key Terms
معلول محض
علة مستقلة
سلسلة
استقلال
داخل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Independence (istiqlal) means that the cause acts entirely alone, without any cooperation from any other cause.
Correction: In this context, independence only means not needing assistance from an external cause; internal causation is allowed.

Checkpoint
What does 'independence' (istiqlal) mean in the answer?
A) Not needing any cause at all
B) Not needing assistance from an external cause
C) Being self-sufficient without parts
D) Being the first cause in the series

Key Takeaway
The final effect can be an independent cause as long as 'independence' is understood as freedom from external assistance, not absolute self-causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The final effect does not cause itself—it is brought into existence by an internal cause. Are you living as if your own existence and actions are self-caused? Commit to one way you will remind yourself daily that your being is entirely from Allah.

Third Objection and Answer on the Whole as a Single Series
Addresses objection of missing last effect in infinite series.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection of a missing last effect in an infinite series is answered by identifying the cause as the infinite preceding collections.

Core Idea
The infinite series is caused by its infinite preceding part.

This passage presents a third objection against the coherence of an infinite causal series: if there is no last effect, how can the series have a cause? The answer shifts the perspective from the whole to its parts, grounding causality in the infinite collections that precede. Understanding this resolution clarifies how infinite causal chains can be logically consistent without requiring an external stopping point.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection: 'If one takes the whole... then this objection is also repelled, because there is no last effect.' Understand that the objector claims the lack of a final effect undermines the previous refutation.
Notice the objector assumes the whole must have an external cause.
2
Analyze the answer
Read the answer: 'Rather, its cause is made the part which is the infinite collections that preceded.' Identify that the cause is internal to the series, not external.
The 'part' refers to the infinite preceding collections, not the whole series.
3
Connect to the concept of infinite causality
Reflect on how this solution preserves causality within an infinite chain without requiring a first cause. The infinite series is self-caused through its parts.
This is a key nuance in kalam arguments about causal regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إذا أخذت الجملة أعني من أن تكون سلسلة واحدة أو سلاسل غير متناهية على ما ذكرتم، فهذا المنع أيضاً مندفع ، إذ ليس هناك معلول أخير، ومجموع مرتب قبله . قلنا : بل وأراد بأن يجعل علتها الجزء الذي هو المجموعات الغير المتناهية التي قبل كان معلولاتها الأخيرة لغير المتناهية .
If it is said: If one takes the whole, meaning whether it is a single series or infinite series as you mentioned, then this objection is also repelled, because there is no last effect and an ordered totality before it. We say: Rather, its cause is made the part which is the infinite collections that preceded, whose last effects are infinite.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
The objector argues that if the whole is a single series or infinite series, then the previous objection fails because there is no last effect (ma'lul akhir) in an infinite chain. The answer states that the cause of the whole is the part consisting of the infinite preceding collections, and these collections have no final effect—they are themselves infinite. Thus the causal chain remains coherent without an external cause.

Key Terms
جملة
سلسلة غير متناهية
معلول أخير
مجموعات غير متناهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An infinite causal series cannot have any cause because there is no first or last effect.
Correction: The cause can be internal to the series, specifically the infinite preceding collections that serve as the cause of the subsequent whole.

Checkpoint
What does the answer identify as the cause of the whole?
A) An external transcendent cause
B) The last effect in the series
C) The part consisting of infinite preceding collections
D) Nothing, the whole is uncaused

Key Takeaway
An infinite causal series does not require an external cause if its cause is the infinite part from which it proceeds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The objector in the passage tried to escape the need for a cause by denying a last effect. In your life, do you ever use the complexity or endlessness of a situation as an excuse to avoid acknowledging a higher cause? What specific situation comes to mind? Commit today to tracing one chain back to its true source.

Fourth Objection and Answer on Priority of Parts
Refutes the claim that no part of the whole has priority to be its cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the cause of a composite whole must be a specific part rather than any arbitrary part.

Core Idea
The part immediately before the last effect is uniquely determined as the cause.

In this objection-and-answer passage, al-Sanusi tackles a subtle point about causality in composite wholes. The objector claims that no part can be the cause because no part has priority. The reply identifies a specific part—the one preceding the last effect—as uniquely suited to be the cause. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage carefully. Identify the two reasons given: no priority of any part, and that a cause of a part would be more influential.
Note the logical structure: 'because no part has priority' and 'because every part you suppose as having a cause is more deserving.'
2
Understand the reply
The reply starts with 'قلنا ممنوع' (we say: denied). Then it states that the part before the last effect (ما قبل المعلول الأخير) is determined for causality.
This part is unique because other parts cannot independently bring the whole into existence.
3
Grasp the reasoning
Why must it be that specific part? Other parts lack independent efficaciousness. Only the part that immediately precedes the final effect can serve as the cause because it is the last in the causal series before the whole is produced.
This connects to the broader theme of causality in the text.
4
Apply to the objection
Reflect on how the reply addresses both objections. It shows that priority is not arbitrary but determined by the causal sequence.
The objector assumed priority must be arbitrary, but the reply shows a definite priority based on the structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : نحن نقول من الابتداء علة الجملة لا يجوز أن تكون جزءاً منها لعدم أولوية بعض الأجزاء أو لأن كل جزء يفرض فعلته أولى منه بأن تكون علة للجملة لكونها أكثر تأثيراً . قلنا ممنوع بل الجزء الذي هو ما قبل المعلول الأخير متعين للعلية، لأن غيره من الأجزاء لا يستقل بإيجاد الجملة على ما لا يخفى .
If it is said: We say from the beginning that the cause of the whole cannot be a part of it because no part has priority, or because every part you suppose as [having] a cause is more deserving to be a cause of the whole since it has more influence. We say: This is denied; rather, the part that is before the last effect is determined for causality, because other parts do not independently bring the whole into existence, as is clear.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
The objector argues that the cause of the whole cannot be a part of it because no part has priority over another, and a cause of a part would be more influential. The answer denies this, stating that the part immediately preceding the last effect (ma qabl al-ma'lul al-akhir) is uniquely determined for causality because other parts cannot independently bring the whole into existence. This establishes a specific internal part as the cause.

Key Terms
علة الجملة
جزء
أولوية
متعين للعلية
ما قبل المعلول الأخير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: No part of a whole can be its cause because no part has inherent priority.
Correction: The part immediately before the last effect is uniquely determined as the cause because it alone can independently bring the whole into existence.

Checkpoint
Which part is determined for causality of the whole?
A) The first part in the series
B) The last effect itself
C) The part before the last effect
D) Any arbitrary part

Key Takeaway
The cause of a composite whole is not arbitrary; it is the part that directly precedes the last effect in the causal sequence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The answer states that other parts do not independently bring the whole into existence. Think of an area of your life—your iman, your relationships, or your work—where you rely on many factors but neglect the one that directly leads to the outcome. The Whole Does Not Need a Distinct Cause
Objection against the need for an external cause for the whole.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the whole does not require an external cause beyond the causes of its individual parts.

Core Idea
The whole's existence is not separate; it is just the individuals together.

This objection challenges the idea that a collection of things needs a separate cause. The author argues that as long as each individual has its own cause, there is no need for an additional cause for the collection itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the compact reasoning style.
The passage is an objection to the idea that a whole needs a separate cause.
2
Identify the objection's claim
Find where the author states they do not accept the whole needing a cause other than the individuals' causes.
The key phrase is 'أنا لا نسلم افتقار الجملة المفروضة إلى علة غير علل الآحاد'.
3
Understand the condition
The objection says a separate cause would only be needed if the whole had an existence distinct from the individuals. Consider what that means.
The phrase 'وجود مغاير' is crucial—distinct existence.
4
Reflect on the ten men analogy
The analogy of ten men illustrates that the group does not need an extra cause—each man has his own cause.
This reinforces that the whole is reducible to its parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى أصل الدليل منع آخر، وهو أنا لا نسلم افتقار الجملة المفروضة إلى علة غير علل الآحاد . وإنما يلزم لو كان لها وجود مغاير لوجود ذات (۱) الآحاد المعللة كل منها بعلته . وقولهم : إنها ممكن مجرد عبارة بل هي ممكنات تحقق كل منها بعلته . فمن أين يلزم الافتقار إلى علة أخرى؟ وهذا كالعشرة من الرجال لا يفتقر إلى غير علل الآحاد وما يقال أن وجودات الآحاد غير وجود كل منها كلام خال عن التحصيل (۲)
And based on the original proof, there is another objection: that we do not accept that the supposed whole needs a cause other than the causes of the individuals. It would only be necessary if it had an existence distinct from the existences of the individuals (1), each of which is caused by its cause. Their saying that it is possible is mere words; rather, it is possible things each of which is realized by its cause. So from where does the need for another cause arise? This is like ten men not needing [a cause] beyond the causes of the individuals, and the claim that the existences of the individuals are other than the existence of the whole is talk devoid of verification (2).
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents another objection: the whole does not need a cause beyond the causes of the individuals (al-ahad). It would only require a separate cause if the whole had an existence distinct from the individuals' existences. The objector argues that calling the whole 'possible' is just a label; each individual is possible and caused individually. The analogy of ten men illustrates that the group's existence is not additional. The claim of distinct existence is unsubstantiated.

Key Terms
جملة
آحاد
وجود مغاير
ممكنات
علل الآحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The whole has a single existence over and above its parts.
Correction: The objector denies this; the whole does not have a separate existence—it is just the individuals together.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, when would the whole need a separate cause?
A) If the whole has no cause
B) If the individuals are uncaused
C) If the whole had an existence distinct from the individuals
D) If the individuals are infinite

Key Takeaway
The whole does not need a separate cause because it does not have an existence distinct from the individuals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes view your life as a single 'whole' that needs a grand, separate cause—like a perfect plan or a single decisive moment—rather than seeing it as the sum of daily, individually caused actions? How does this perspective affect your sense of personal accountability for each small deed?
?
If the whole does not have an existence distinct from its parts, then your character is nothing but the collection of your individual choices. Are you relying on a 'good overall person' label while neglecting the need to correct each specific habit that contradicts that label? What concrete step will you take today to fix one individual action?
?
The objection dismisses the idea that the whole needs a separate cause. Yet in your worship, do you sometimes seek a single 'spiritual experience' to justify your faith, while ignoring that faith is built on individual acts of obedience? What is one individual act you have postponed that, if done, would cause your faith to be more consistent?

The Proof by Cutting and Applying Against Infinite Regress
A description of the proof by cutting and applying against infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof by cutting and applying (برهان القطع والتطبيق) and how it demonstrates the impossibility of an infinite causal regress.

Core Idea
The proof by cutting and applying shows that an infinite series leads to a contradiction.

This lesson introduces a powerful logical tool used by theologians to disprove the possibility of an infinite chain of causes. Known as the proof by cutting and applying, it relies on comparing parts of a series to show that an infinite sequence cannot exist. The author presents a concise argument that you will unpack step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to get the gist of the argument.
Notice that the passage includes margin notes in parentheses.
2
Understand the cutting step
Focus on how we separate a 'whole' from the series by removing one element from its end. This creates two series: the original (complete) and the truncated (incomplete).
3
Understand the applying step
Now we compare the two series part by part. This is the 'application' (تطبيق). Pay attention to the two possible outcomes.
The comparison assumes a one-to-one correspondence of parts.
4
Analyze the two outcomes
If every part corresponds, the whole equals its part (contradiction). If not, the incomplete series must end, implying the complete series also ends (finiteness).
Both outcomes lead to the impossibility of an infinite series.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand why this proof is used in theology to argue that causal chains must be finite, thus requiring a First Cause (Allah).
This proof assumes the series is ordered (e.g., causes).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان القطع والتطبيق على استحالة التسلسل
التامة (٦) جزء من
الناقصة
(قال) : الثاني (۳) نفصل من السلسلة (( جملة بنقصان واحد من طرفها (٥) ، ثم نطبق بين الجملتين فإن وقع بإزاء كل جزء من لزم تساوي الكل والجزء وإلا لزم انقطاع الناقصة وتناهي التامة بالضرورة (٧) حيث لا تزيد عليها إلا بواحد (۸) .
The proof of cutting and applying on the impossibility of infinite regress. The complete (6) part of the incomplete. He said: The second (3): We separate from the series a whole by removing one from its end (5), then we compare between the two wholes; if every part corresponds to a part, then the whole equals the part, otherwise the incomplete is interrupted and the complete necessarily ends (7), since it does not exceed it except by one (8).
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage describes the proof of 'cutting and applying' (qat' wa tatbiq) to demonstrate the impossibility of an infinite regress. First, from an assumed infinite series, we cut a whole (jmla) by removing one element from its end. Then we compare (apply) the two wholes: if every part of one corresponds to a part of the other, the whole and part are equal (contradicting the whole being larger); if not, the truncated (incomplete) series must end, implying the original complete series also ends, as it is only one element more. The footnotes may contain additional explanations.

Key Terms

سلسلة
ناقصة
تامة
تطبيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof by cutting and applying is about numerical series.
Correction: It is actually about causal chains where order matters.

Checkpoint
In the proof, what is done to the series before comparing?
A) It is doubled in length
B) One element is removed from its end
C) It is divided into two equal parts
D) The order is reversed

Key Takeaway
The proof by cutting and applying shows that an infinite chain of causes leads to a logical contradiction, thus proving it impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on countless causes and means every day. If you trace your own existence back step by step, are you living as if that chain is infinite and self-sufficient, or have you truly anchored your trust in the First Cause who alone requires no cause?
?
The proof shows that an infinite regress leads to absurdity. In your life, are there patterns of excuses or dependencies that form an endless chain—'I will change when...'—that you are using to avoid confronting the ultimate reality of standing before Allah?
?
If the series of causes must end in a Necessary Being, what is one area of your life where you are still acting as if you can be independent of that Being? How will you cut that chain today?

The Proof of Cutting and Application: Objection and Resolution
Presents the first version of the proof of cutting and application with objection and resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first version of the proof of cutting and application, its objection from examples like numbers and divine knowledge, and the resolution that restricts the proof to actual existents with real order.

Core Idea
Comparing an infinite chain to one missing a part shows that infinite regress is impossible for actual existents.

Sanusi presents a logical demonstration against infinite regress by comparing two allegedly infinite chains. However, the proof faces objections from examples like numbers and divine knowledge. He then clarifies the scope of the argument, restricting it to actual existents with real order.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation slowly. Note the key terms: السلسلة (chain), التامة (complete), الناقصة (deficient), التطبيق (application).
The proof is very concise; each phrase carries weight.
2
Understand the logical mechanism
Focus on the two possibilities: either every part of the complete chain corresponds to a part of the deficient chain (leading to equality of whole and part, which is impossible), or not (leading to the deficient chain stopping and the complete chain being finite).
This is the core of the proof.
3
Examine the objection
Identify the examples given: numbers, God's knowledge, celestial movements, etc. Understand why these seem to contradict the proof.
The objection claims the proof would incorrectly apply to these infinite series.
4
Grasp the resolution
Note the distinction between actual existence and imaginary/mental existence. The proof applies only to series with actual existence and real order, not to purely mental ones.
The intellect's role is crucial; general consideration suffices, but detailed observation would never complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان القطع والتطبيق على استحالة التسلسل
التامة (٦) جزء من
الناقصة
(قال) : الثاني (۳) نفصل من السلسلة (( جملة بنقصان واحد من طرفها (٥) ، ثم نطبق بين الجملتين فإن وقع بإزاء كل جزء من لزم تساوي الكل ،والجزء وإلا لزم انقطاع الناقصة وتناهي التامة بالضرورة (٧) حيث لا تزيد عليها إلا بواحد (۸) ، ونوقض أصل الدليل بسلسلة الأعداد عند الكل، ومعلومات الله تعالى عندنا وحركات الأفلاك عند الفلاسفة، ولزوم انقطاع الناقصة بتضعيف الواحد مراراً غير متناهية مع تضعيف الاثنين كذلك. ومقدورات الله تعالى ومعلوماته، ودورات زحل مع دورات القمر. وحاصله أنه يجوز أن يكون بإزاء كل جزء جزء لعدم تناهيهما لا لتساويهما . فإن سمى مثله (۱) تساوياً مع إستحالته، ووجه التقصي (٢) دعوى الضرورة، وتخصيص الحكم فعندنا بما دخل تحت الوجود إذ الوهمي ينقطع بانقطاع الوهم وعندهم (۳) بماله مع الوجود بالفعل ترتب وضعاً أو طبعاً، إذ يمتنع التطبيق فيما عداه . والحق أن اعتبار الأثنينية والتطبيق إنما هو بحسب العقل . فإن اكتفى بعرض العقل إجمالاً قام () في الكل، وإن اشترط الملاحظة تفصيلاً لم يتم أصلا.
The proof of cutting and application on the impossibility of infinite regress. The complete [chain] (6) part of the deficient [chain]. He said: The second [way] (3): we separate from the chain a set [by] missing one from its end (5), then we apply [comparison] between the two sets. If for every part of the complete a part [of the deficient] falls in line, then equality of the whole and the part follows; otherwise, the deficient [chain] necessarily stops and the complete [chain] ends (7) since it does not exceed it except by one (8). And the original proof was objected to by the series of numbers according to all, and by God's knowledge according to us, and by the movements of the heavens according to the philosophers, and by the necessary stopping of the deficient [chain] by doubling one infinitely many times with doubling two likewise, and by God's capabilities and knowledge, and by Saturn's revolutions with the Moon's revolutions. Its conclusion is that it is permissible that for every part [of one chain] there is a part [of the other] because both are infinite, not because they are equal. If he calls such [correspondence] equality while it is impossible, and the way of escape (2) is the claim of necessity, and the specification of the ruling according to us is for what enters under existence, for the imaginary stops with the stopping of the imagination, and according to them (3) for what has actual existence, order by position or nature, for application is impossible in other than it. The truth is that consideration of duality and application is only by the intellect. If it is satisfied with the intellect's general presentation, it stands in everything; if detailed observation is required, it is never completed.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents the first version of the proof of cutting and application (برهان القطع والتطبيق) used to demonstrate the impossibility of infinite regress (استحالة التسلسل). The author describes the proof: we take an assumed infinite chain (السلسلة) and separate from it a set by removing one element from its end, resulting in two chains: the complete (التامة) and the deficient (الناقصة). Then we apply (نطبق) between them, meaning we compare them element by element. If every element of the complete chain corresponds to an element of the deficient chain, then the whole and part would be equal, which is absurd. If not, then there must be an element of the complete that has no corresponding element in the deficient, meaning the deficient chain terminates, and the complete chain is only one element longer, so it must also be finite. The author then raises an objection: this proof would also apply to the series of natural numbers (which are infinite), God's knowledge (infinite), the philosophers' celestial movements, etc., leading to contradictions. In response, the author explains that the proof only applies to things with actual existence and a real order, not to imaginary or mental entities. The true consideration of duality and application is by the intellect: if the intellect suffices with a general notion, it applies everywhere; if detailed observation is required, it fails.

Key Terms
برهان القطع والتطبيق
استحالة التسلسل
السلسلة
التامة
الناقصة
التطبيق
تساوي الكل والجزء
انقطاع الناقصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of cutting and application applies to any infinite series, including numbers, God's knowledge, and celestial movements.
Correction: The proof only applies to series that have actual existence and a real order (by position or nature). Purely imaginary or mental series are not subject to the proof.

Checkpoint
According to the proof of cutting and application, what happens if every part of the complete chain corresponds to a part of the deficient chain?
A) The deficient chain becomes infinite.
B) Equality of the whole and part follows, which is impossible.
C) The complete chain ends.
D) The proof fails.

Key Takeaway
The proof of cutting and application demonstrates that an infinite regress of actual causes is impossible, but it does not apply to purely conceptual or imaginary series.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely daily on causes—your alarm clock waking you, your food nourishing you. But what if you truly believed that each of these causes ends at Allah's direct action, with no endless chain? Would you still feel anxiety about things not going as planned? Pause and identify one situation this week where you acted as if an infinite chain of causes was necessary. What would it mean to cut that chain mentally and see only Allah's hand?
?
The proof says that imaginary infinite series (like numbers) don't disprove the impossibility of actual infinite regress. Reflect: Have you ever excused your own lack of trust in Allah by thinking 'it's just how the world works'? That 'how the world works' is an imaginary series? Right now, can you name one real event in your life where you can trace its cause back to Allah and stop there, without needing a further cause? What stops you from living in that reality?
?
This lesson argues that the intellect's general consideration is enough to know that infinite regress is impossible. But do you actually live as if your intellect has settled that matter? Or do you still feel a deep-seated assumption that things just happen because of endless circumstances? Write down one area of your life where you behave as if causes have no ultimate end. Now, consciously affirm: 'Allah is the Creator of this cause and its effect.' What would it look like to truly believe that?

Second Version of the Proof of Application and the First Objection
Presents the second version of the proof of application and begins an objection based on numbers and divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the second version of the proof of application and the first objection that argues it would wrongly imply the finitude of numbers and God's knowledge.

Core Idea
The proof of application is meant to prove finitude, but its own logic would force absurd consequences.

This passage introduces the second and more relied-upon version of the proof of application (برهان التطبيق). The proof attempts to show that any chain ending in a pure cause must be finite. However, the author immediately presents an objection: if the proof were sound, it would imply that natural numbers and God's knowledge are finite — both of which are false. We will walk through the proof's reasoning and then examine the first objection, though the passage cuts off mid-sentence.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's description
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the setup: an infinite chain to a pure cause, removal of one element, and the formation of two sets (complete and deficient).
The proof relies on matching parts between the two sets.
2
Understand the contradiction
If every part of the complete has a corresponding part in the deficient, then the whole equals a part (impossible). If not, the deficient stops and the complete is only one more, so it is finite.
This is the core of the proof.
3
Follow the objection
The objector argues that the same reasoning would force numbers (from 1 and from 2) to be finite, and also God's knowledge and movements to be finite. The passage breaks off while listing these consequences.
The objection is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : ويسمى برهان التطبيق وعليه التعويل، في كل ما يدعي تناهيه ، (٥) أنه لو وجدت سلسلة غير متناهية إلى علة محضة تنقض طرفها المتناهي واحد فتحصل جملتان إحداهما من المعلول المحض، والثانية من الذي فوقه ثم تطبق بينهما، فإن وقع بإزاء كل جزء من التامة لزم تساوي الكل والجزء وهو محال، وإن لم يقع ولا يتصور ذلك إلا بأن يوجد جزء من التامة لا يكون بإزائه جزء من الناقصة لزم انقطاع الناقصة بالضرورة. والتامة لا يزيد عليها إلا بواحد على ما هو المفروض، فيلزم تناهيها ضرورة، أن الزائد على المتناهي بالمتناهي متناه واعترض بوجهين . أحدهما : نقض أصل الدليل بأنه لو صح لزم أن تكون الأعداد متناهية ، لأنا نفرض جملة من الواحد إلى غير النهاية، وأخرى من الاثنين إلى غير النهاية ، ثم نطبق بينهما ، وتناهي الأعداد باطل بالاتفاق، وأن تكون معلومات الله تعالى متناهية للتطبيق بين الكامل وبين الناقص منه بواحد وتناهيها باطل عند المتكلمين وأن تكون الحركات
The second aspect: it is called the proof of application and it is relied upon, in everything claimed to be finite. (5) That if there existed an infinite chain to a pure cause, its finite end is removed by one, resulting in two sets: one of pure effect, and the second of what is above it, then apply between them. If for every part of the complete a part falls in line, then equality of the whole and part follows, which is impossible. If not, and that is only conceivable by there being a part of the complete that has no corresponding part of the deficient, then the deficient necessarily stops. And the complete does not exceed it except by one, as supposed, so its finitude necessarily follows, for that which exceeds the finite by the finite is finite. And it was objected to in two ways. One of them: a refutation of the original proof, for if it were valid, numbers would have to be finite, for we assume a set from one to infinity, and another from two to infinity, then apply between them; and the finitude of numbers is invalid by consensus; and that God's knowledge would be finite by applying between the complete and the deficient by one, and its finitude is invalid according to the theologians; and that the movements [would be finite].
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents the second version of the proof of application (الوجه الثاني من برهان التطبيق), which is relied upon for proving finitude. The author describes a scenario: suppose an infinite chain (سلسلة غير متناهية) leading to a pure cause (علة محضة). Remove one element from its finite end, resulting in two sets: one of pure effect (المعلول المحض) and one of what is above it (الذي فوقه). Apply between them. Again, if every part of the complete corresponds to a part of the deficient, equality of whole and part occurs (impossible). Otherwise, a part of the complete lacks a counterpart in the deficient, so the deficient necessarily stops, and the complete is only one more, thus finite. The author then records an objection with two points. The first objection (أحدهما) is a refutation of the original proof: if this proof were sound, it would force the natural numbers (which are universally agreed to be infinite) to be finite, because one can take the series from 1 to infinity and from 2 to infinity and apply the same reasoning. Similarly, it would force God's knowledge (infinite) to be finite, and celestial movements to be finite. The text ends mid-sentence, indicating the objection continues on a subsequent page.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of application is the same as the first version of the proof.
Correction: The second version specifically targets chains ending in a pure cause (علة محضة) and is considered more reliable.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The second version of the proof of application attempts to prove finitude by matching parts of two sets, but the first objection shows it would lead to absurdities like finite numbers and finite divine knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In your own life, are you relying on any 'proofs' about ultimate matters that might break down if you thought more carefully about their assumptions? What would it mean to hold those beliefs with humility?

Response to the Second Objection: Infinite Series and Necessity of Equality
Taftazani refutes an objection about comparing infinite series of causes and effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani refutes the objection that comparing infinite series implies termination of the lesser, and why the distinction between mental entities and actual existents is crucial.

Core Idea
Infinity does not behave like finite numbers; equality or inequality of infinites does not imply termination.

In this lesson, we examine a powerful objection to the premise that if one infinite series is less than another, the lesser must terminate. The objector presents examples like multiplying one versus two infinitely, or God's powers versus His knowledge. Taftazani then offers a precise answer, distinguishing between things that exist in actuality versus mental constructs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow from objection to answer.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objection and its examples
The objector claims that comparing two infinite series necessarily leads to equality or termination. Find the three examples given (multiplication, God's powers vs. knowledge, Saturn vs. moon revolutions).
These examples challenge the original premise.
3
Understand Taftazani's response
Taftazani specifies the ruling: impossibility only arises when one number is truly above another, not between two infinites. He then distinguishes between 'our view' (Islamic) and 'the philosophers' view' regarding actual existence.
Notice the key distinction: mental considerations vs. actual simultaneous ordered existence.
4
Reflect on theological implications
Consider how this defense preserves the possibility of infinite divine attributes and causal chains without logical contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما: نقض المقدمة القائلة بأن إحدى الجملتين إذا كانت أنقص من الأخرى لزم انقطاعها بأن الحاصل من تضعيف الواحد مراراً غير متناهية أقل من تضعيف الاثنين مراراً غير متناهية مع لا تناهيها اتفاقاً، ومقدورات الله تعالى أقل من معلوماته لاختصاصه بالممكنات وشمول العلم للممتنعات أيضاً مع لا تناهي المقدورات عندنا ودروات زحل أقل من دورات القمر ضرورة مع لا تناهيها عند الفلاسفة وحاصل الاعتراض أنا نختار أنه يقع بإزاء كل جزء من الناقصة، ولا نسلم لزوم تساويهما. فإن ذلك كما يكون للتساوي . فقد يكون لعدم التناهي، وإن سمى مجرد ذلك تساوياً. فلا نسلم إستحالة ذلك فيما بين التامة والناقصة بمعنى نقصان شيء من جانبها المتناهي، وإنما يستحيل ذلك في الزائدة والناقصة بمعنى كون عدد إحداهما فوق عدد الأخرى، وهو ليس بلازم فيما بين غير المتناهيين وإن نقصت ألوف. وقد يجاب عن المنع بدعوى الضرورة في أن كل جملتين إما متساويتان أو متفاوتتان بالزيادة والنقصان وأن الناقصة يلزمها الانقطاع وعن النقض يتخصص الحكم. أما عندنا فيما دخلت تحت الوجود سواء كانت مجتمعة كما في سلسلة العلل والمعلولات أو لا كما في الحركات الفلكية، فإنها المعدات فلا ترد الأعداد لأنها من الاعتبارات العقلية، ولا يدخل في الوجود من المعدودات إلا ما هي متناهية، وكذا معلومات الله تعالى ومقدوراته، ومعنى لا تناهيها، أنه لا تنتهي إلى حد لا يكون فوقه عدد أو معلوم أو مقدور آخر . وأما عند الفلاسفة فيما تكون موجودة معاً بالفعل مترتبة وضعاً كما في سلسلة المقادير على ما يذكر في تناهي الأبعاد أو طبعاً كما في سلسلة العلل والمعلولات فلا ترد الحركات الفلكية، لكونها متعاقبة غير مجتمعة ولا جزئیات نوع واحد كالنفوس الناطقة على تقدير عدم تناهيهما بحسب العدد لكونها غير مترتبة .
And the second: a refutation of the premise saying that if one of the two series is less than the other, then its termination is necessary; because what results from multiplying one an infinite number of times is less than multiplying two an infinite number of times, despite both being infinite by agreement; and the powers of God Almighty are less than His knowledge because He is specialized to possibles while knowledge encompasses impossibilities as well, despite the infinity of powers in our view; and the revolutions of Saturn are less than the revolutions of the moon necessarily, despite their infinity according to the philosophers; and the gist of the objection is that we choose that there corresponds to every part of the deficient [series] [a part], and we do not concede that they are necessarily equal. For that may be due to equality, or it may be due to infinity, even if that mere infinity is called equality. So we do not concede the impossibility of that between the complete and the deficient in the sense of a deficiency of something from its finite side. Rather, that is impossible between the exceeding and the deficient in the sense that the number of one is above the number of the other, and that is not necessary between two infinites even if one is deficient by thousands. And it may be answered to the prohibition by claiming necessity that every two series are either equal or different by increase or decrease, and that the deficient necessarily terminates, and concerning the refutation, the ruling is specified. As for in our view, regarding what enters under existence, whether they are simultaneous as in the chain of causes and effects or not as in the celestial movements, they are preparations, so numbers are not considered because they are mental considerations, and only finite numbers enter into existence from counted things, and likewise the knowledge and powers of God Almighty, and the meaning of their infinity is that they do not end at a limit beyond which there is no number or known or power. And as for in the view of the philosophers, regarding what exists actually together ordered positionally as in the chain of magnitudes according to what is mentioned in the finitude of dimensions, or naturally as in the chain of causes and effects, so the celestial movements are not considered because they are successive not simultaneous, nor the individuals of one type like the rational souls on the assumption of their infinity in number because they are not ordered.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The author (Taftazani) presents a second refutation (نقض) of a premise used in an argument about infinite regress. The premise claimed that if two series are infinite and one is less than the other, the lesser must terminate. To refute this, the objector gives examples: multiplying one infinitely yields a smaller infinite than multiplying two infinitely; God's powers are less than His knowledge yet both infinite; Saturn's revolutions are less than the moon's yet both infinite. The objection chooses to assert that correspondence occurs part-by-part without equality being necessary—lack of equality may be due to infinity itself. So impossibility only arises when one number is truly above another, not between two infinites. Then the author gives the proper answer: the ruling is specified. In our (Islamic) view, actual existence only applies to finite counted things; numbers are mental, so infinite series in existence are not actual infinite sets. For the philosophers, only things actually coexistent and ordered (like chains of causes) are considered; successive things like celestial movements or unnumbered individuals like souls are not subject to the argument.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite series can be compared in magnitude even if they are not actually existent or ordered.
Correction: The text explains that numbers are mental considerations; actual existence only applies to finite counted things. For philosophers, only simultaneously existing ordered series are comparable.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why does the argument about infinite series not apply to numbers?
A) Because numbers are finite in reality.
B) Because numbers are mental considerations and do not have actual existence.
C) Because numbers are infinite, so they are beyond comparison.
D) Because numbers are only used in arithmetic, not in causality.

Key Takeaway
Taftazani's response shows that comparing infinite series is only problematic when the series are actual, simultaneous, and ordered; otherwise, infinity does not entail termination or equality.

Objection and Answer on the Application of the Comparison Proof
Al-Taftazani answers the objection that the tatbiq proof is limited in scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Taftazani refutes the objection that specification of the tatbiq proof invalidates it, and why the proof remains valid for all types of series according to the truth.

Core Idea
Specification does not invalidate a proof; it clarifies its scope.

Al-Taftazani now addresses a critical objection: if the tatbiq proof only works for certain series, doesn't that show it's limited and therefore invalid? He responds by distinguishing between a proof being invalid and its scope being specified. This passage explains why the proof applies universally when understood correctly.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial answer
Start by reading the objection that specification implies invalidity, and Al-Taftazani's clarification that it simply means the proof does not apply to certain cases like textual evidence.
Notice the key term 'تخصيص' (specification) and how it is redefined.
2
Examine the two perspectives
The passage presents two views: the mutakallimun (us) and the philosophers (them) on the scope of the tatbiq proof. Compare their positions regarding numbers, celestial motions, and souls.
The mutakallimun hold a broader application than the philosophers.
3
Understand the truth (al-Haqq)
Al-Taftazani declares that the correct view is that the comparison is made by the mind, not externally. The mind's judgment suffices for the proof to apply to all types of series.
This resolves the objection by showing the proof's universality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : التخصيص في الأدلة العقلية، اعترف ببطلانها حيث يتخلف المدلول عنها . قلنا : معناه أن الدليل لا يجري في صورة النص، بل يختص بما عداها . أما عندنا فنظراً إلى أن ما لا تحقق له في نفس الأمر لا يمكن التطبيق فيه إلا بمجرد الوهم فينقطع بانقطاعه بخلاف ما في نفس الأمر، فإنه لا بد أن يقع بإزاء كل جزء جزء، أو لا يقع، وهو معنى الانقطاع، وأما عندهم فنظراً إلى أن التطبيق بحسب نفس الأمر، إنما يتصور فيما له مع الوجود ترتب ليوجد بإزاء تلك، فلا يجري في الأعداد، ولا في الحركات الفلكية، ولا في النفوس الناطقة والحق أن تحصيل الجملتين من جزء من تلك، إنما هو بحسب العقل دون الخارج، فإن كفى في تمام الدليل حكم العقل، بأنه لا بد أن يقع بإزاء كل جزء جزء أو لا يقع . فالدليل جار في الأعداد وفي الموجودات المتعاقبة، والمجتمعة المترتبة وغير المترتبة، لأن للعقل أن يفرض ذلك في الكل، وإن لم يكن ذلك، بل اشتراط ملاحظة أجزاء الجملتين على التفصيل لم يتم الدليل في الموجودات المترتبة فضلا عما عداها لأنه لا سبيل للعقل إلى ذلك إلا فيما لا يتناهى من الزمان .
If it is said: 'Specification in rational proofs is an admission of their invalidity since the conclusion does not follow from them.' We say: Its meaning is that the proof does not apply to the case of the text, but is specific to what is beyond it. As for us, it is because something that has no actual existence cannot have application except by mere imagination, so it ceases when the imagination ceases, unlike what exists in actuality, for it must either correspond to each part or not, which is the meaning of cessation. As for them, they consider that application in terms of actual existence is only conceivable for something that has a causal order in existence so that it corresponds to those [parts]; thus it does not apply to numbers, nor to celestial motions, nor to rational souls. But the truth is that the comparison of the two series from a part of that is only by the mind, not externally. If the mind's judgment suffices for completing the proof, namely that it must either correspond to each part or not, then the proof is valid for numbers, for successive existents, and for simultaneous ones, whether ordered or unordered, because the mind can assume that in all, even if that is not the case. Rather, requiring detailed observation of the parts of the two series would not complete the proof for ordered existents, let alone others, because the mind has no way to do that except in what is infinite in time.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer regarding the scope of the comparison (tatbiq) proof against infinite regress. The objector claims that if the proof is specified (takhsis) to certain types of series, that is an admission of its invalidity because the intended conclusion would not follow. The teacher Al-Taftazani responds by clarifying that specification does not mean the proof is invalid; it means the proof does not apply to exceptional cases. He then explains two perspectives: ours (the mutakallimun) and theirs (the philosophers). According to the mutakallimun, the tatbiq proof works because for actual existents, the mind necessarily judges that either each part is matched or not. For philosophers, the proof only works for ordered series; it does not work for numbers, celestial motions, or souls. But the truth (al-Haqq) is that the comparison is performed by the mind, not externally. If the mind's judgment is sufficient, the proof is valid for all types of series. If one requires detailed observation of every part, the proof fails even for ordered series except where the mind can grasp the infinite, which is impossible. Thus the objection is refuted.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that specification (takhsis) of a proof to certain cases automatically invalidates it.
Correction: Specification only narrows the scope; it does not undermine the proof's validity when applied to its proper domain.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, why does the bit-tatbiq proof apply to numbers even though they are not causally ordered?
A) Because numbers are actual existents.
B) Because the mind can assume correspondence for any two series.
C) Because numbers are temporal.
D) Because philosophers accept it.

Key Takeaway
The tatbiq proof is not invalidated by specification; when the mind's judgment is sufficient, it applies universally.

The Third Proof Against Infinite Regress: Equality of Correlatives
Al-Taftazani presents the third proof and two formulations based on equality of correlatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof against infinite regress based on the necessary equality between cause and effect in a causal chain.

Core Idea
In any causal series, a pure cause must exist to preserve the equality of correlatives.

Al-Taftazani presents the third argument against infinite regress, which relies on the logical relationship between correlatives like cause and effect. This argument shows that an infinite chain would violate the necessary mutual dependence of these correlatives. The passage introduces the core idea and two formulations.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the quoted statement within parentheses.
2
Identify the two formulations
The passage mentions two formulations: one based on counting causes and effects, the other on comparing sets of causality and effectuality. Find each.
The first formulation uses 'increase in number'; the second uses 'comparison between two sets'.
3
Reflect on why equality forces termination
Ask yourself why the equality of correlatives (cause and effect) requires the series to have both a pure cause and a pure effect. How does an infinite series break equality?
Think about the last effect (pure effect) and the first cause (pure cause).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث من استحالة التسلسل (قال : الثالث لما اشتملت السلسلة على معلول محض لزم اشتمالها على علة محضة تحقيقاً لتكافؤ المتضايفين، فينقطع، وهذا مأخذ العبارات منها لو تسلسلت العلل لزم زيادة عدد المعلول على عدد العلة ضرورة أن كل ما هو علة فيها، فهو معلول من غير عكس فيبطل التكافؤ ومنها تطبيق بين جملتي العلية والمعلولية في تلك السلسلة، فإن تفاوتا بطل التكافؤ، وإلا لزم علية بلا معلولية ضرورة أن في الجانب المتناهي معلولية بلا علية) . الوجه الثالث : أنه لو لم تنته سلسلة العلل والمعلولات إلى علة لا يكون معلولاً لشيء لزم عدم تكافؤ المتضايفين، واللازم باطل لما سيجيء، أو نقول : لو كان المتضايفان متكافئين لزم انتهاء السلسلة إلى علة محضة، والمقدم حق لأن معناه أنهما بحيث إذا وجد أحدهما في العقل أو في الخارج وجد الآخر، وإذا انتفى انتفى . وجه اللزوم أن المعلول الأخير يشتمل على معلولية محضة، وكل مما فوقه على علية ومعلولية، فلو لم ينته إلى ما يشتمل على علية محضة لزم معلولية بلا علية .
The third proof concerning the impossibility of infinite regress. (He said: The third: Since the series includes a pure effect, it necessarily includes a pure cause to achieve the mutual equality of the two correlatives, so it terminates. And this is the source of the formulations: among them, if causes regress infinitely, the number of effects would exceed the number of causes necessarily because every cause in it is an effect, not conversely, thus the equality would be nullified. And among them, a comparison between the two sets of causality and effectuality in that series; if they differ, equality is nullified; otherwise, a causality without effectuality would be necessary, because in the finite side there is effectuality without causality.) The third proof: If the chain of causes and effects does not terminate at a cause that is not an effect of anything, then the non-equality of the two correlatives would be necessary, and the consequent is false as will come. Or we say: If the two correlatives are equal, then the series must terminate at a pure cause. The premise is true because its meaning is that they are such that if one exists in the mind or in the external world, the other exists, and if one is negated, the other is negated. The reason for the entailment is that the last effect involves pure effectuality, and everything above it involves causality and effectuality. So if it does not terminate at what involves pure causality, then effectuality without causality would be necessary.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument (al-wajh al-thalith) against the possibility of an infinite regress of causes and effects. The argument is based on the principle of the mutual equality of correlatives (takaful al-mutadayifayn), such as cause and effect. The passage begins by quoting the original text: 'The third: since the series includes a pure effect (an effect that is not a cause), it must include a pure cause (a cause that is not an effect) to achieve the equality of correlatives, so the series terminates.' Then it expands on this. Two formulations are given: first, if causes regress infinitely, the number of effects would be greater than the number of causes (since every cause is also an effect except the first), violating equality. Second, a comparison between the two sets of causality and effectuality in the infinite series: if they are not equal, equality is violated; if they are equal, then since the finite side (the last effect) has effectuality without causality, the other side must have causality without effectuality for equality, implying a pure cause. Then the teacher restates the argument: If the chain does not end at a pure cause, then correlatives are not equal, which is false. Alternatively: if correlatives are equal, the series must end at a pure cause. The premise is true because correlatives by definition imply each other's existence in mind or reality. The final explanation: the last effect has pure effectuality; each above has both causality and effectuality. If no pure cause exists, then somewhere effectuality would be without causality, breaking equality. Therefore the regress is impossible.

Key Terms
وجه
تسلسل
علة محضة
معلول محض
تكافؤ المتضايفين
علية
معلولية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think an infinite regress is possible if each cause is also an effect, so no need for a first cause.
Correction: The argument shows that such a chain would violate the equality of cause and effect because a pure effect (the last) forces a pure cause to balance it.

Checkpoint
What does the 'third proof' argue must exist in a causal chain to satisfy the equality of correlatives?
A) An infinite number of causes.
B) A pure cause that is not an effect.
C) A pure effect that has no cause.
D) A circular loop of causes.

Key Takeaway
The third proof uses the necessary equality of correlatives to demonstrate that an infinite causal regress is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a blessing, do you instinctively thank Allah as the Pure Cause, or do you stop at the immediate means (your effort, another person)? Commit to tracing every good back to Allah and thanking Him first.
?
If you believe that every effect must have a cause, examine your life: in what areas do you live as if you are independent—relying on your own power, as if you were a self-caused cause? What would it look like to live in constant awareness of being a pure effect dependent on Allah?
?
The argument shows that a chain of causes cannot exist without a First Cause. Do you treat Allah as that First Cause in your daily decisions, or do you act as if secondary causes are sufficient? What is one habit you can change today to acknowledge that nothing happens except by Allah's causation?

The Third Proof: Necessity of a Pure Cause for Equality
Al-Taftazani restates the proof and explains the necessity of a pure cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the equality of correlatives demands that the causal chain ends at a pure cause.

Core Idea
If correlatives are equal, the series must end at a pure cause; otherwise, inequality results.

The passage restates the third proof in a more formal manner, showing that the failure to terminate at a pure cause would disrupt the equality of cause and effect. It explains that the last effect is a pure effect, and everything above it is both cause and effect, so the series must begin with a pure cause to maintain the mutual dependence.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. This part restates the argument with a conditional and an alternative formulation.
Note the phrase 'or we say' indicating an alternative approach.
2
Understand the definition of correlatives
The passage defines correlatives: if one exists, the other exists; if one is negated, the other is negated. This is key to the argument.
Correlatives are mutually dependent by definition.
3
Why the chain must end at a pure cause
The last effect is a pure effect; every link above is both cause and effect. Without a pure cause, effectuality would appear without a cause, breaking equality.
Focus on the final sentence: 'effectuality without causality would be necessary'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Now, think of the most profound blessing in your life — something you had no hand in creating. ?

How would acknowledging the Pure Cause behind everything reshape your relationship with your Lord?
?

But do you live as though you are self-sufficient, as though you are not an effect? When you make plans, do you consider that you are caused by the Pure Cause every moment? Make a conscious decision to say 'I am dependent' and let that soften your heart before you sleep tonight.

Equivalence of Cause and Effect: Two Formulations Against Infinite Regress
Refutation of an objection and two formulations of the proof against infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the equivalence of causality and effecthood is used in two distinct formulations to prove the necessity of a first cause.

Core Idea
For every effect there must be a cause; that very equivalence demands a first cause.

This passage addresses a potential objection to the proof from equivalence: if every effect needs a cause, why does that require a first cause? The author clarifies the intended meaning and then presents two crisp formulations that drive the point home. Each formulation shows that an infinite chain violates the necessary one-to-one correspondence between causes and effects.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Start by reading the objection that the equivalence of cause and effect does not require a pure cause. Then follow the author's concise answer.
Note how the answer rephrases the equivalence as 'for every effecthood there must be a causality'.
2
Study the first formulation
Work through the first formulation: infinite chain leads to more effects than causes. Understand why every cause in the chain is also an effect, but not every effect is a cause.
3
Study the second formulation
Now examine the second formulation: take groups of causalities and effects, and compare them. See how both equality and inequality lead to contradictions unless the chain ends in a pure cause.
Pay attention to the subtlety of the 'final effect' at the bottom of the chain.
4
Reflect on the necessity of a first cause
Both formulations converge on the same conclusion: an infinite regress is impossible, so there must be a first cause that is pure cause and not effect.
This is a cornerstone argument in kalam for the existence of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : المكافىء لمعلولية المعلول المحض علية المعلول الذي فوقه بلا وسط لا علية العلة المحضة. قلنا : نعم إلا أن المراد أنه لا بد أن يكون بإزاء كل معلولية علية . وهذا يقتضي ثبوت العلة المحضة. وللقوم في التعبير عن هذا الاستدلال عبارتان : إحداهما لو تسلسلت العلل والمعلولات إلى غير النهاية لزم زيادة عدد المعلول على عدد العلة وهو باطل ضرورة تكافؤ العلية والمعلولية . وبيان اللزوم أن كل علة في السلسلة فهو معلول على ما هو المفروض، وليس كل ما هو معلول فيها علة كالمعلول الأخير. وثانيهما: نأخذ جملة من العليات التي في هذه السلسلة، وأخرى من المعلوليات، ثم نطبق بينهما، فإن زادت آحاد إحداهما على الأخرى بطل تكافؤ العلية والمعلولية لأن معنى التكافؤ أن يكون بإزاء كل معلولية علية ، وبالعكس، وإن لم تزد لزم علية بلا معلولية ضرورة أن في الجانب المتناهي معلولية بلا علية كما في المعلول الأخير، فلزم الخلف لأن التقدير عدم انتهاء السلسلة إلى علة محضة.
If it is said: The equivalence (al-mukāfiʾ) for the effecthood of the pure effect is the causality of the effect above it without an intermediary, not the causality of the pure cause. We say: Yes, but what is meant is that there must be a causality corresponding to every effecthood. And this necessitates the establishment of the pure cause. And for the people there are two expressions for this proof: One of them: If causes and effects were to form an infinite chain (tasalsal), then the number of effects would exceed the number of causes, which is invalid due to the necessary equivalence (takāfuʾ) of causality and effecthood. And the explanation of the implication is that every cause in the chain is an effect according to what is assumed, and not every thing that is an effect in it is a cause, like the final effect. And the second: We take a group of the causalities in this chain, and another from the effects, then we compare them; if the units of one exceed the other, the equivalence of causality and effecthood is invalidated, because the meaning of equivalence is that corresponding to every effecthood there is a causality, and vice versa; and if they do not exceed, then a causality without an effecthood necessarily follows, because in the finite side there is an effecthood without a causality, as in the final effect, so a contradiction follows because the assumption is that the chain does not terminate at a pure cause.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by two formulations of a proof against infinite regress (tasalsul). The objection claims that the equivalence (mukāfiʾ) for the effecthood of the pure effect is the causality of the effect above it, not the pure cause. The answer clarifies that the intended meaning is that for every effecthood there must be a corresponding causality, which necessitates a pure cause. Then the author gives two expressions of the proof. The first expression argues that if causes and effects formed an infinite chain, the number of effects would exceed the number of causes, contradicting the necessary equivalence (takāfuʾ) of causality and effecthood. The explanation is that every cause in the chain is also an effect, but not every effect is a cause (e.g., the final effect). The second expression takes two sets: one of causalities and one of effects from the chain. They are compared: if the units of one exceed the other, equivalence fails; if they are equal, then on the finite side there is an effect without a cause (the final effect), contradicting the assumption that the chain does not end in a pure cause. Both formulations demonstrate the necessity of a first cause.

Key Terms
تكافؤ العلية والمعلولية
علة محضة
تسلسل
العلل والمعلولات
معلول محض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The equivalence of causality and effecthood only requires that every effect has an immediate cause, not a first cause.
Correction: The equivalence requires a one-to-one correspondence across the entire chain, which forces the existence of a pure cause that is not itself an effect.

Checkpoint
In the second expression of the proof, what happens if the units of causalities and effects in the chain are equal?
A) The chain continues infinitely.
B) A causality without an effecthood necessarily follows.
C) An effecthood without a causality necessarily follows, like in the final effect.
D) The equivalence is preserved.

Key Takeaway
Both formulations demonstrate that an infinite chain of causes and effects violates the necessary equivalence of causality and effecthood, proving the existence of a first cause that is pure cause.

The proof shows that without a pure cause, the chain of causality collapses into contradiction. What step will you take today to realign your heart with that reality?

The Fourth Proof: Isolating the Pure Effect
The author presents the fourth argument by treating each individual under two aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth kalamic proof against infinite regress, which compares the chain of causes and the chain of effects to show a necessary increase in causality that forces termination.

Core Idea
Comparing causes and effects reveals an extra cause — proving the chain must end.

This lesson presents the fourth and perhaps most subtle argument against infinite regress. It does not simply count entities but multiplies each intermediate being into two aspects: as cause and as effect. By comparing these two chains, a mismatch emerges that forces a first cause. Let's follow this careful reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the concise proof statement
Read the opening lines where the author states the proof: isolate the pure effect, treat each individual under two aspects, then compare causes and effects. Notice the conclusion: causality increases and chains end.
The proof is stated very compactly; the explanation follows.
2
Understand the two aspects
A thing as cause is different from itself as effect. This yields two separate sequences. Ask yourself why this distinction is necessary for the argument.
Without this distinction, we would just be counting individual entities.
3
See the mismatch in ranks
Every cause corresponds to the effect of its prior cause, not its own effect. So the chain of causes has one extra member—a cause that is not an effect. That terminates the regress.
The final effect is not a cause, so the cause chain has one more rank.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع من إبطال التسلسل: الرابع: نعزل المعلول المحض ونجعل كلا من الآحاد متعدداً باعتبار وصفي العلية والمعلولية، ثم نطبق بين سلسلتي العلل والمعلولات فيلزم بضرورة سبق العلة زيادة العلية ويتناهيان. وبيانه: أنا ننزل المعلول المحض من السلسلة المفروضة، ونجعل الآحاد التي فوقه متعدداً باعتبار وصفي العلية والمعلولية لأن الشيء من حيث إنه علة مغاير له من حيث إنه معلول، فتحصل جملتان متغايرتان بالاعتبار إحداهما العلل والأخرى المعلولات، ويلزم عند التطبيق بينهما زيادة وصف العلية ضرورة سبق العلة على المعلول، فإن كل علة لا تنطبق على معلولها في مرتبتها، بل على معلول علتها المتقدمة عليها بمرتبة خروج المعلول الأخير لعدم كونه مفروضاً للعلية فيلزم زيادة مراتب العلل بواحدة وإلا بطل السبق اللازم للعلة. ومعنى زيادة مرتبة العلية أن يوجد علة لا تكون معلولاً وفيه انقطاع للسلسلتين.
The fourth aspect of invalidating infinite regress: The fourth: We isolate the pure effect and make each of the individuals multiple with respect to the two attributes of causality and effecthood, then we apply [comparison] between the two chains of causes and effects; then necessarily, due to the priority of the cause, the causality increases, and both chain end. And its explanation: We remove the pure effect from the supposed chain, and make the individuals above it multiple with respect to the two attributes of causality and effecthood, because a thing qua cause is different from itself qua effect. Thus two distinct groups arise by consideration: one group of causes and another of effects. And upon comparison between them, an increase in the attribute of causality necessarily follows due to the priority of the cause over the effect; for every cause does not correspond to its effect in its rank, but rather to the effect of its cause that precedes it by one rank, except for the exit of the final effect because it is not assumed for causality. Thus the ranks of causes increase by one; otherwise the priority necessary for cause is invalidated. And the meaning of the increase in the rank of causality is that there exists a cause that is not an effect, and in that is an interruption of both chains.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof against infinite regress. The author first states the proof: we isolate the pure effect (the final effect that is not a cause) and treat each individual in the chain as having two aspects — one qua cause and one qua effect. Then we compare the two chains (the chain of causes and the chain of effects). Because cause precedes effect, the number of causalities must exceed the number of effects, forcing both chains to end. The explanation clarifies: we remove the pure effect from the chain and consider the remaining individuals under two aspects. Since a thing as cause is different from itself as effect, we obtain two distinct sets. When we compare them, there is an increase in the attribute of causality because every cause in the chain corresponds not to its own effect but to the effect of its prior cause, except for the final effect (which is not a cause). This mismatch forces an extra rank of causality, meaning a cause that is not an effect — which terminates both chains.

وصف العلية والمعلولية
تطبيق بين سلسلتي العلل والمعلولات
سبق العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this argument is about counting individual entities, but it is about counting aspects.
Correction: Each intermediate entity is counted twice: once as cause and once as effect. The mismatch is not in entities but in the ranks of causality.

Checkpoint

A) The number of effects exceeds the number of causes.
B) The number of causes exceeds the number of effects by one.
C) The numbers are equal.
D) The chains go on infinitely.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof shows that comparing the chains of causes and effects yields an extra cause, proving a first cause that is not an effect, thus halting infinite regress.
