The Fifth Proof against Infinite Regress: Even/Odd Parity
The author presents and then refutes a proof based on parity in division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the even/odd parity argument against infinite regress and its refutation.

Core Idea
Infinity defeats the even/odd classification of a series.

Sanusi presents the fifth kalam argument against infinite regress, based on whether an infinite series can be divided into even or odd parts. The argument seems compelling but has a subtle flaw: the very assumption that the series can be categorized as even or odd presupposes finitude. In this lesson, we examine both the argument and its crucial refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text and its literal English translation to get the author's own wording before interpreting.
Notice the concise style; the refutation is only a short clause.
2
Understand the logical structure of the proof
The proof argues: if the series can be divided into two equal parts, it is even; otherwise odd. Then it notes that odd numbers are one less than the following even, and vice versa, implying finiteness.
The proof assumes the series must be either even or odd.
3
Examine the refutation
The refutation states that the failure to divide (i.e., not being classifiable as even/odd) may be due to infinity itself, not finiteness.
This challenges the premise that every series can be evenly or oddly divided.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this refutation is important for kalam: it shows that intuitive parity arguments do not apply to infinite series.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس من إبطال التسلسل: الخامس: تلك السلسلة إن انقسمت بمتساويين فزوج وإلا ففرد، وكل زوج أقل بواحد من فرد بعده وبالعكس فتتناهى. ورد بأن عدم الانقسام قد يكون لعدم التناهي.
The fifth aspect of invalidating infinite regress: The fifth: That series — if it is divided into two equals, it is even; otherwise it is odd. And every odd is one less than the even that follows it, and vice versa, so it is finite. And it is refuted by [the objection] that non-division may be due to infinity.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof against infinite regress, followed by its refutation. The proof argues that the series (of causes and effects) is either divisible into two equal parts (making it even) or not (making it odd). Moreover, every odd number is one less than the following even number, and vice versa. This pattern implies that the series must be finite (because if it were infinite, it would not have these parity properties). The refutation (ward) counters that the failure of division (i.e., the series not being explicitly even or odd) could be due to the series being infinite, not finite. Thus the argument is not conclusive.

Key Terms
زوج
فرد
انقسام
عدم التناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument about even/odd proves the series must be finite.
Correction: The argument assumes the series can be divided into even/odd categories, which is only possible if the series is finite; an infinite series may not be classifiable that way.

Checkpoint
What is the refutation (ward) against the fifth proof?
A) The series is always even.
B) The series is always odd.
C) Non-division may be due to infinity.
D) The proof is valid and no refutation exists.

Key Takeaway
The parity argument fails because infinite series cannot be meaningfully classified as even or odd.

The Fourth Proof against Infinite Regress: Increasing Ranks of Causality
Sanusi presents the fourth proof against infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's fourth proof against infinite regress, which shows that in any chain of causes and effects, the number of causes must be one more than the number of effects, thus making the chain finite.

Core Idea
Causes outnumber effects by one, breaking infinite regress.

In this lesson, we explore a subtle but powerful argument that demonstrates the impossibility of an infinite chain of causes and effects. By carefully distinguishing between the aspects of being a cause and being an effect, Sanusi reveals a necessary asymmetry that forces the chain to terminate. This proof is a classic example of philosophical reasoning in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once for overall meaning, then follow the English translation. Notice how the argument proceeds step by step.
The key terms are 'علة' (cause) and 'معلول' (effect).
2
Distinguish the two aspects
Understand that every link in the chain (except the last) can be considered both as a cause and as an effect. Sanusi separates these two descriptions.
This yields two distinct sequences: one of causes, one of effects.
3
Map the sequences
When you align the cause-sequence with the effect-sequence, each cause corresponds to an effect one rank earlier. The last effect has no corresponding cause because it is a pure effect.
This mismatch is the heart of the proof.
4
Draw the conclusion
The necessary increase in the number of causes (by one) means there is a cause that is not an effect—breaking the chain and proving it must be finite.
Infinite regress is impossible.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why this proof is considered decisive: it relies only on the logical priority of cause over effect, without any extra assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : أنا ننزل المعلول المحض من السلسلة المفروضة، ونجعل الآحاد التي فوقه متعدداً باعتبار وصفي العلية والمعلولية لأن الشيء من حيث إنه علة مغاير له من حيث إنه معلول، فتحصل جملتان متغايرتان بالاعتبار إحداهما العلل والأخرى المعلولات، ويلزم عند التطبيق بينهما زيادة وصف العلية ضرورة سبق العلة على المعلول، فإن كل علة لا تنطبق على معلولها في مرتبتها، بل على معلول علتها المتقدمة عليها بمرتبة الخروج المعلول الأخير لعدم كونه مفروضاً للعلية فيلزم زيادة مراتب العلل بواحدة وإلا بطل السبق اللازم للعلة . ومعنى زيادة مرتبة العلية أن يوجد علة لا تكون معلولاً وفيه انقطاع للسلسلتين .
The fourth aspect: We take the pure effect from the supposed chain, and we consider the units above it as multiple by virtue of the two descriptions of causality and effect-ness, because a thing inasmuch as it is a cause is different from it inasmuch as it is an effect. Thus two groups differing in consideration are obtained: one of causes and the other of effects. And when comparing them, an increase in the description of causality necessarily follows due to the priority of the cause over the effect. For every cause does not correspond to its effect in its rank, but rather to the effect of its cause which precedes it by one rank, except for the last effect because it is not considered for causality. Thus an increase in the ranks of causes by one necessarily follows; otherwise the priority necessary for causality would be invalid. And the meaning of the increase in the rank of causality is that there exists a cause that is not an effect, and therein lies a break in both chains.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof against infinite regress (tasalsul). The argument proceeds by considering a supposed infinite regress of causes and effects. The author first takes a pure effect (the last effect in the chain) and then considers each of the preceding units as having two aspects: being a cause (علة) and being an effect (معلول). These two aspects are distinct in consideration. Based on this, we obtain two sequences: one of causes and one of effects. When we map them onto each other, we notice a necessary increase in the number of causes because each cause, by its essence, must be prior to its effect by one rank. Therefore, the cause in one rank does not correspond to its effect in the same rank but to an effect one rank earlier. The only exception is the last effect (the pure effect) which has no causal aspect. This forces an extra rank of cause beyond the chain of causes and effects, thus breaking the supposed infinite regress. The conclusion is that the chain must be finite.

Key Terms
تسلسل
علة
معلول
زيادة المراتب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument adds a cause arbitrarily, but it logically follows from the priority of cause over effect.
Correction: The extra cause is not assumed; it is a logical consequence of the fact that each cause must be prior to its effect, so when aligning the sequences, the causes necessarily outnumber the effects.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, what happens when we compare the sequence of causes to the sequence of effects?
A) They match exactly in number.
B) The number of causes is one less than the number of effects.
C) The number of causes is one more than the number of effects.
D) The sequences are infinite.

Key Takeaway
An infinite regress of causes is impossible because the ranks of causes necessarily exceed the ranks of effects by one, forcing a finite end.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every effect must have a cause and the chain cannot go on forever, your existence is directly tied to the First Cause who is not an effect. When do you most feel self-sufficient, and what would it take for you to actually feel your complete dependence on that First Cause in those moments?
?
The proof forces a break in the chain—a cause that is not an effect. Consider an area of your life where you keep looking for 'reasons' or 'explanations' that never lead back to God. What is one specific area, and what would it mean for you to stop that regress and submit to the ultimate Cause?
?
Sanusi shows that even in a supposed infinite chain, logic demands an original cause. Have you ever used phrases like 'it's just how the world works' or 'things happen for a reason' to avoid confronting that your entire existence is a direct effect of the First Cause? The Fifth Proof: Parity Argument and Its Refutation
Sanusi presents the parity argument against infinite regress and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the parity argument against infinite regress and why it fails because parity applies only to finite numbers.

Core Idea
Even and odd are properties of finite numbers, so the parity argument cannot prove an infinite series finite.

The fifth proof against infinite regress tries to use the mathematical properties of evenness and oddness to force finitude. Sanusi presents this argument and then refutes it by showing that parity itself presupposes finitude. This passage exemplifies how careful logical critique uncovers hidden assumptions in seemingly powerful proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and its structure
Read the passage carefully, noting that the proof treats the causal chain as a number. It argues that the number must be even or odd, and each case leads to finitude.
The proof is presented twice in the passage; focus on the more detailed version.
2
Identify the refutation
The refutation points out that the assumption that 'not divisible into two equal parts implies odd' is only valid for finite numbers. For an infinite series, the failure to divide may be due to infinity itself.
Also note the alternative refutation that every number is less than another, which also assumes finitude.
3
Understand the key insight
The core insight is that evenness and oddness are properties exclusive to finite numbers. Therefore, applying them to an infinite series is a category mistake.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس من إبطال التسلسل: تلك السلسلة إن انقسمت بمتساويين فزوج وإلا ففرد، وكل زوج أقل بواحد من فرد بعده وبالعكس فتتناهى. ورد بأن عدم الانقسام قد يكون لعدم التناهي. ثم قال: الوجه الخامس: أن السلسلة المفروضة من العلل والمعلولات الغير المتناهية إما أن تكون منقسمة بمتساويين فيكون زوجاً أو لا فيكون فرداً، وكل زوج فهو أقل بواحد من فرد بعده كالأربعة من الخمسة وكل فرد فهو أقل بواحد من زوج بعده كالخمسة من الستة، وكل عدد يكون أقل من عدد آخر يكون متناهيا بالضرورة. كيف لا؟ وهو محصور بین حاصرين هما ابتداؤه وذلك الواحد الذي بعده. ورد بأنا لا نسلم أن كل ما لا ينقسم بمتساويين فهو فرد، وإنما يلزم لو كان متناهياً، فإن الزوجية والفردية من خواص العدد المتناهي، وقد يطوي حديث الزوجية والفردية، فيقال: كل عدد فهو قابل للزيادة فيكون أقل من عدد فيكون متناهياً والمنع ظاهر.
The fifth proof for refuting infinite regress: That chain, if it is divisible into two equal parts, then it is even; otherwise it is odd. And every even is less by one than an odd after it, and vice versa, so it is finite. It is refuted by that lack of division may be due to infinity. Then he said: The fifth proof: That the supposed chain of causes and effects that is infinite is either divisible into two equal parts, so it is even, or not, so it is odd. And every even is less by one than an odd after it, like four from five; and every odd is less by one than an even after it, like five from six. And every number that is less than another number is necessarily finite. How not? For it is bounded between two bounds: its beginning and that one after it. It is refuted by that we do not grant that everything that is not divisible into two equal parts is odd; that would only be necessary if it were finite. For evenness and oddness are properties of the finite number. And the discussion of evenness and oddness may be avoided, so it is said: Every number is susceptible to increase, so it is less than a number, so it is finite. But the denial is obvious.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof against infinite regress and its refutation. The proof argues that the infinite chain of causes and effects, considered as a number, must be either even or odd. If it is even, it is less by one than an odd number that comes after it; if it is odd, it is less by one than an even number. In either case, it is less than another number, therefore finite. The first refutation points out that the inability to divide into equal parts may be due to infinity, not oddness. The second refutation (from the elaborated version) denies that every indivisible into equal parts is odd, because that applies only to finite numbers. Evenness and oddness are intrinsic to finite numbers. Alternatively, one might argue directly that every number is less than another number because it is susceptible to increase, which also only holds for finite numbers. Thus the proof fails.

Key Terms
زوج
فرد

غير متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The parity argument proves that an infinite causal chain is finite.
Correction: Parity only applies to finite numbers, so the proof fails for infinite series. Evenness and oddness are not defined for infinite quantities.

Checkpoint
What is the main refutation of the fifth proof based on parity?
A) Infinite series cannot be divided at all.
B) Evenness and oddness only apply to finite numbers.
C) The proof confuses cause and effect.
D) The chain is not a number.

Key Takeaway
The parity argument fails because evenness and oddness are properties of finite numbers only, and thus cannot be applied to an infinite series.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to understand Allah's attributes, do you ever unknowingly apply finite human concepts like 'even' or 'odd' to Him? What specific concept have you used that might be just as inappropriate?
?
This lesson shows that a logical argument can seem convincing but fail because it assumes finitude. Are there areas in your faith where you rely on your own reasoning more than revelation? How will you humble yourself before Allah's infinite nature?
?
The refutation warns against forcing finite categories onto the infinite. In your daily life, do you try to put Allah in a box—expecting Him to act according to your limited understanding? What is one way you can let go of that expectation and trust His infinite wisdom?
